# Danielle Cage  —  *Danielle Cage*

![[_attachments/danielle_cage.png]]

# Danielle Cage — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

## Bottom Line, Up Front

Danielle Cage is, at the deepest level, **a child raised at the operational center of a permanent crisis by two people who loved her as competently as trauma survivors can love anything — and who, in the timeline that matters most, did not survive long enough to see what she became without them.** She is the proof-of-concept of her parents' marriage: the artifact that demonstrates the Cage-Jones household, for all its sirens and stash-houses and rotating superhuman babysitters, succeeded at the one thing it was built to do. She is also the bill that household ran up, paid forward into a future where the people who raised her are gone and the symbol she carries is the only place the grief has anywhere to go.

She is not a legacy character in the conventional sense — not a daughter playing dress-up in inherited iconography. She is a load-bearing replacement: a person whose entire developmental architecture was, without anyone planning it, a training program for the role of last-one-standing. The training took. That is the headline. Everything else is the cost.

## The Through-Line

One sentence, longitudinal: **she was conceived inside a siege, born into a siege, raised inside a siege, and in her worst future she became the siege's last competent answer.**

The continuity is unbroken across every era of her published existence. As an infant she is handed between superhuman caregivers because her parents are needed elsewhere; as a toddler she is the named reason her parents accept or refuse missions; as an adult, in the timeline where both are dead, she takes up not her father's identity-of-power but a borrowed shield representing a principle larger than any of them. At each developmental stage the same structural fact recurs: **she is the thing the adults around her are protecting, and the thing whose existence justifies the protecting.** The role of *reason* and the role of *responsibility* fuse early. By adulthood they are indistinguishable inside her — she does not protect because she was protected; she protects because protection is what a person *is*.

The through-line is not trauma. It is **inheritance under pressure**. Her parents inherited their lives back from the systems that took them (Seagate; the Purple Man) and built a household out of what was left. She inherited that household, intact, and then the future took it from her, and she built a *role* out of what was left. The pattern is generational: take what survives, make a structure of it, hold the line.

## Who She Is at the Deepest Level

Strip away the shield, the powers, the time-displacement, the costume, the cohort: the residue is **a competent, watchful, warmly-available person whose default posture toward the world is custodial.** She is the kind of person who counts the room when she enters it. She is the kind of person who knows where every member of her team is at all times without making a show of knowing. She is the kind of person who can hold a child or a weapon with the same calibrated steadiness because both were modeled for her early by parents who treated tenderness and tactical readiness as two registers of the same competence.

She is, in the deepest substrate, **her mother's vigilance fused with her father's stillness, organized around a principle her father admired and her mother distrusted on her behalf.** Jessica gave her the scanner; Luke gave her the keel; Steve Rogers' tradition gave her a shape to pour both into. None of the three is sufficient alone. The combination is what she is.

She is also, importantly, **not anyone's surrogate.** She is not a small Jessica; she is not a female Luke; she is not Steve Rogers' heir in any spiritual sense. The canon is careful to render her as her own composition. She carries the shield without the theology that animated its previous bearer — she believes in people, not in nations, not in symbols-as-such, not in a constitutional ideal. The shield is a tool. The principle is the people inside the perimeter. This is her mother's pragmatism wearing her father's calm wearing a borrowed flag.

## The Core Wound

The core wound is **not the loss of her parents** — that is the precipitating injury in the adult timeline, but it is not the wound. The wound is older and structural: **she was raised to understand that the people who loved her were not reliably available to her, because the same competence that made them lovable made them needed elsewhere.** Her early caregiving environment was extravagantly loving and chronically interrupted. Squirrel Girl, Jarvis, Jessica Drew, Wolverine, Iron Fist — these are not babysitters one engages because the parents want a night out. These are deputies one engages because the parents are at war. A child does not parse this as abandonment; a child parses it as *the way safety works*. Safety is a roster. Love is a roster. Family is a roster.

This is the wound: **the internalized model that intimacy is distributed, that the dyad is not load-bearing, that to be loved is to be loved by a team that is also at war.** It is not a deficit of love. It is a structural relocation of love — out of the home and into the network. In adulthood this presents as a strength: she integrates into Da Costa's unit with seamless trust, treats teammates as kin within days, and operates with an emotional fluency that most orphans do not possess. It also presents as the place she does not look: **she has no model for love that is not also a mission, no template for an intimacy that does not have a perimeter.** She has never been shown attempting one, and would, if pressed, likely not know where to start.

The loss of her parents in the future timeline did not create this wound. It *confirmed* it. The roster-model of safety was vindicated by their deaths: they were, indeed, not reliably available, and eventually not available at all. Her response — joining the largest possible roster, taking the largest possible shield, becoming the deputy of a tradition rather than the daughter of a family — is the wound writing its own prescription.

## How the Wound Is Maintained

The maintenance system is elegant and nearly invisible, which is why it has held through every stress depicted in canon. Four mechanisms:

1. **Role-as-container.** The Captain America identity is not a job she does; it is the structure inside which her grief is permitted to exist without overwhelming her. The shield organizes the day. The mission organizes the week. The principle organizes the life. So long as the role holds, the grief is held. The role is therefore not negotiable.

2. **Forward-motion defense.** She does not relitigate. She does not ruminate. Her cognitive style is pragmatic and clean — pattern, decide, execute, next. This is partially inherited (Jessica's PI economy; Luke's street decisiveness) and partially adaptive: a person whose history would, if examined, decompensate them is a person who does not examine. Forward motion is the form her avoidance takes, and because forward motion is also the form her competence takes, the defense is indistinguishable from the virtue.

3. **Distributed attachment.** By spreading trust across a network rather than collapsing it onto a single figure, she protects herself from the catastrophic dyadic loss her parents' deaths represented. No single person can be lost catastrophically because no single person carries the full weight. This is functional and humane — she is a generous teammate, a steady presence — and it is also the wound continuing to write the rules.

4. **Symbolic over-identification.** Taking the shield is the introjective mourning solution par excellence: *become the thing you cannot have back.* But what she introjected was not her parents — it was the *principle* her parents served, abstracted one level up. This is sophisticated: it gives her something to be that is larger than the people she lost, which protects her from the impossible task of replacing them. It is also a deferral. The grief for the principle is bearable. The grief for the man and the woman who taught her to brush her teeth has not been addressed and, structurally, cannot be addressed inside the container she has built.

The maintenance is so well-engineered that **the system will hold indefinitely under conditions of continued mission.** It is engineered to fail only under conditions of *peace*, *role-loss*, or *forced intimacy outside the perimeter*. None of these has occurred in canon. All of them remain latent.

## Operational Summary

**Asset class.** Top-tier enhanced operator. Inherited super-strength at or above paternal baseline; inherited durability sufficient to absorb enhanced-opponent blunt force without sequelae; trained in shield combat to a competence the canon presents as natural rather than effortful. Tactically literate, command-capable, time-displacement-adapted. Functions equivalently as solo operator, team member, or team lead.

**Reliability.** Exceptionally high. No substance use, no impulse-control concerns, no documented ethical lapses, no record of insubordination that is not principled. Will execute lawful orders aligned with her value-set; will refuse and articulate refusal of orders that violate it. Predictable in the specific sense that her values are stable and visible.

**Interpersonal profile.** Warm, available, professionally close, quick to build working trust. Disclosure is slow and selective. Treats team as family and means it; will absorb personal cost to protect the unit. Romantic and non-mission peer intimacy are undeveloped competencies — not deficits of capacity, but of practice. Probable preference for partners encountered inside operational contexts.

**Stress profile.**
- *Acute:* composes, prioritizes, executes. Affect compresses. Performance under acute stress is at or above baseline.
- *Chronic:* probable somatic routing (sleep disruption, appetite, social withdrawal from non-mission contact). Body does not break; pressure goes elsewhere. Will not self-report.
- *Catastrophic:* loss of a team-family member under her command is the highest-leverage stressor; would test the introjective grief solution that has held to date. Loss of the role itself (forced retirement, loss of the shield, peacetime) is the second.

**Pressure points (ranked).**
1. The present-day living parents she is not letting herself fully reach for. The largest single piece of unprocessed material in her current configuration.
2. The unexamined fusion of role and identity. Threats to the role read, internally, as threats to the self.
3. A teammate's death under her command.
4. Forced inactivity. The container requires the world to keep needing her; in a world that does not, she would have to face what is inside the container.
5. Sustained one-to-one intimacy outside a mission context. Not a danger but a destabilizer.

**Handling recommendations.** Do not flatter; she registers it and discounts the source. Do not invoke her parents instrumentally; she will tolerate it once and remember. Give her the perimeter and the principle and stand out of her way. If you must give her bad news, give it cleanly and without prelude — she metabolizes direct information well and prelude as manipulation. If she goes quiet, the issue is real; her baseline is warm-professional, and withdrawal is a tell.

**Net assessment.** A securely-attached child of two trauma-survivors became, in her worst-case future, the load-bearing symbol the world needed after losing the people who taught her what loads were worth bearing. She is high-functioning, ethically anchored, mature in defense structure, and free of active disorder. The strength is real and earned. The cost is deferred, contained, and — as of the current record — unexamined. She is what her parents managed to build before the world took them. She is also the standing demonstration that what they built was load-bearing enough to carry the weight of their absence.

That is the file in one breath: **the daughter held; the symbol holds; the grief waits.**


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject: Danielle Cage. Period covered: conception through approximately age four-to-five in published time, i.e., the present-day-MU child record from* The Pulse *#13 (2006) through the late-2010s family-unit appearances. The adult/future-timeline material is reserved for Chronology II; only inferences from the child record that bear directly on the formation of that later subject are surfaced here.*

---

## 1.1 Conception, Gestation, Natal Period

**Conception window.** Dani was conceived in the late post–*Alias*/early-*Pulse* period, in the eighteen-month interval during which Jessica Jones and Luke Cage moved from cohabitation into pregnant cohabitation and then into a shotgun-style civil ceremony performed under the implicit pressure of impending parenthood (*The Pulse* #11–13, 2006). The relationship was, at point of conception, less than two years stabilized after a long mutual history of intermittent contact, one prior sexual encounter of opportunistic character (the Birch Street/Luke-Cage’s-apartment night referenced retrospectively across *Alias*), and a courtship conducted in counterpoint to Jessica's reconstruction from the Killgrave captivity. The pregnancy was, on Jessica's own statement, not strictly planned but not refused — a decision-by-not-deciding, characteristic of both parents' approach to large life pivots.

**Gestation environment.** Jessica's pregnancy ran across one of the most operationally hostile windows in the modern MU timeline: the back half of the *Pulse* run, the lead-up to *Civil War*, and the immediate aftermath of *House of M*. Jessica was an employed staff investigator at the *Daily Bugle* (the Pulse desk under Ben Urich and Kat Farrell) through most of the second and third trimester. She was, by her own account and Luke's, not in active costumed combat after the first trimester, but she was repeatedly proximate to combat: the Green Goblin attack on the *Bugle* offices (*Pulse* #1–5 arc residue), the Nick Fury black-bag operation that culminated in the secret war fallout, and the day-to-day exposure of being Luke Cage's pregnant partner in a Harlem brownstone that was, at that moment, an unofficial Avengers waypoint.

**Pre/perinatal stress load.** High-cortisol gestational environment, sustained. Not teratogenic in any clinically meaningful sense — Jessica's Kree-altered physiology is highly resilient and Dani was carried to term without recorded complication — but the *signal environment* of the pregnancy was unrelenting threat-assessment. The pregnant mother was a person whose nervous system had been trained, by the Killgrave year, to read every room for predators. That reading does not switch off because one is gravid. The fetus matured in a body whose baseline autonomic tone was elevated.

**Birth.** *The Pulse* #13 (2006). Delivery at a Manhattan hospital, with Luke present, attended in part by Dr. Strange in a consulting/protective capacity given the suite of supernatural and superhuman threats that had, in the preceding months, made any high-value birth in New York a potential target. The delivery was complicated by an emergent threat — a recurring pattern that begins, literally, at the natal moment: Dani's first breath is drawn into a room where adults are calculating extraction routes. **Name selection:** "Danielle" honoring Danny Rand (Iron Fist), Luke's partner of longest standing and the figure whose loyalty across the wrongful-imprisonment period made him the *de facto* brother-in-law of the household. The name is a declaration of operational kinship — Dani is, from the naming ceremony forward, *of* the Heroes for Hire lineage as much as of the Cage-Jones one.

**No middle name on consistent canonical record;** the household uses "Dani" almost exclusively. The diminutive sticks because both parents are themselves people who shed formal names — Carl Lucas to Luke Cage, Jessica Campbell to Jessica Jones — and the household culture is one in which the lived name is the real one.

---

## 1.2 Family of Origin: Structural Map

**Father — Luke Cage (Carl Lucas).** Born in Harlem, framed and incarcerated at Seagate Penitentiary, subjected non-consensually to the Burstein Process which produced his enhanced physiology, escaped, cleared his name across years of effort, built a working life as a hero-for-hire and a stabilizing presence in Harlem. As a father: tactile, demonstrative, hands-on with diapers and night-feedings, narratively cited in *New Avengers* (Bendis, vol. 1 #50s; vol. 2 entire run) and Walker's *Power Man and Iron Fist* (2016) as the parent who *carries the baby into the meeting*. He does not park her. He brings her. This is structurally important: Luke's experience of paternal absence (his own incarceration-shaped life, the broader pattern of Black fatherhood under American carceral pressure that the books explicitly thematize) produces a paternal practice organized around *presence as defiance*. He is in the room with his daughter, on principle.

**Mother — Jessica Jones-Cage.** Origin: orphaned by the car accident that also produced her latent Kree-derived powers; raised by the Jones family; brief abortive superhero career as Jewel; year of captivity, sexual assault, and coercion under Zebediah Killgrave (the Purple Man); subsequent reconstruction as private investigator (*Alias*) and as a journalist (*The Pulse*). As a mother: ambivalent in language, unambivalent in action. The textual Jessica complains constantly about the difficulty of motherhood, expresses doubt about her fitness, swears in front of the baby — and does not, ever, on the page, fail to act on Dani's behalf. The verbal register is one of self-deprecation; the behavioral register is one of fierce, calculating, frequently illegal protection. *Jessica Jones* (2016, Bendis) opens with her having executed a multi-month elaborate deception — including faking a separation from Luke and the temporary loss of Dani — *as a protective operation*. The mother's love is operationalized as tradecraft.

**The parental dyad as environment.** Luke and Jessica are a complementary pair: he is regulated, slow to anger, ethically anchored, physically immovable; she is dysregulated in expression but disciplined in action, fast, suspicious, profane, loyal. The marital baseline is *bickering with absolute trust* — the household soundtrack is sarcasm over a foundation of unbreakable mutual reliance. A child raised in this dyad learns: *loud is not unsafe; quiet is not safe; what matters is whether they show up*. They show up.

**Extended kin (blood / fictive).**
- **Danny Rand (Iron Fist):** uncle in all but blood. Namesake. A constant presence in the apartment through the *Power Man and Iron Fist* (2016) run; functions as the household's second adult-male presence and as Luke's emotional regulator.
- **Misty Knight:** functional aunt; Dani is in her arms repeatedly across *Heroes for Hire* and family scenes; the Knight-Rand pairing and the Cage-Jones pairing operate as a two-couple kin network through Dani's early years.
- **Edwin Jarvis:** intermittent caregiver during the Avengers Tower residency; the trusted-elder figure. (See §1.4 for the rupture.)
- **Squirrel Girl (Doreen Green):** retained as nanny during the *New Avengers* vol. 2 period under Bendis. Genuinely competent, genuinely warm, and — crucially — combat-rated. The nanny can fight off the Wrecking Crew. This is *normal* in Dani's environment.
- **Jessica Drew (Spider-Woman):** Jessica's closest female peer; functional aunt; semi-frequent presence.
- **Carol Danvers:** godmother-adjacent presence; Jessica's longest peer bond.
- **Wolverine, Iron Fist, Spider-Man, Doctor Strange:** circulating adults during the Avengers Tower / Avengers Mansion residencies, all of whom hold the baby on panel at various points.
- **Maternal grandparents:** the adoptive Jones family, intermittently present; the relationship between Jessica and her adoptive mother is rendered as warm-but-fraught and Dani sees this grandmother occasionally but not as a primary attachment figure.
- **Paternal grandparents:** Luke's father James Lucas Sr. and the late mother — the relationship with the father was reconciled in late *Power Man and Iron Fist* and Cage Sr. becomes a present figure in Dani's toddlerhood after long estrangement.

**The structural inference.** Dani's primary attachment dyad is robust and present but is *embedded* in an unusually large rotating cast of secondary attachment figures, most of whom are superhuman combatants. The norm for her is not *parent or stranger* but *parent or one of fifteen named adults who have held me*. This is the developmental substrate for the later adult pattern of treating teams as kin — the wiring is laid in the crib.

---

## 1.3 Childhood Environment: Domiciles, Routines, Sensory Field

**Domicile 1: The Harlem brownstone / above-the-bar apartment (pre-natal through *New Avengers* vol. 2 displacement).** The bar Luke owned or co-managed in this period, with apartment above, served as the first residence. Street-level, embedded in Harlem, with a clientele that crossed civilians, hero-adjacent locals, and active Heroes for Hire business. **Sensory environment:** street noise, frequent foot traffic in and out of the apartment, irregular hours, music from the bar below at night. **Security posture:** door reinforced, windows reinforced after one incident, Luke routinely armed-by-virtue-of-skin.

**Domicile 2: Avengers Tower nursery (Bendis *New Avengers* and *Mighty Avengers* crossover period, post-*Civil War* into *Secret Invasion*).** When Luke joined the underground / New Avengers in opposition to the SHRA, the family quartered for periods at Doctor Strange's Sanctum Sanctorum and at various safe-houses; later, post-*Siege*, in the rebuilt Avengers Tower with a nursery wing established. **Sensory environment:** institutional, climate-controlled, quiet, vast. The Tower nursery is the safest physical space in the western hemisphere and Dani spent meaningful infant time in it. Jarvis is the dominant ambient adult.

**Domicile 3: Return to street-level Harlem, Walker's *Power Man and Iron Fist* (2016).** With the post-*Siege* normalization, the family decompressed back to a Harlem apartment, this one a more conventional family flat. Routines stabilized: school-prep registration begins, Dani has a room, has toys, has a routine. **This is the most "normal" period of her childhood and lasts the shortest.**

**Daily routine patterns reconstructable from the canon.**
- **Wake:** early; Luke is an early riser, Jessica is not; Dani is woken by Luke for breakfast on the days Luke is home.
- **Feeding:** mixed — Jessica nursed in infancy on what evidence is shown; transitioned early; Luke is the daily cook in the household, with a documented repertoire of breakfast items (eggs, toast — *Power Man and Iron Fist* shows this directly).
- **Daycare / care substitution:** rotating. Squirrel Girl on a regular schedule during the Bendis period; Danny Rand or Misty Knight on call; Jessica's PI office (when she resumed work) brought into use as a child-on-site environment.
- **Bedtime:** Luke does most of the bedtime work shown on page; he reads to her, he carries her to bed, the cadence of this is repeatedly used as a beat in the comics specifically to mark the household's tenderness against its violence.
- **Bath, dress, transitional rituals:** unremarkable on record but present, performed by both parents.

**Sensory baseline of the child.** Dani has, from infancy: been held by enhanced individuals whose body temperature, scent, and tactile signature differ from baseline humans (Luke's subdermal density gives a distinct feel; Logan's particular smell; Jarvis's English-tailored fabric and aftershave; Doctor Strange's incense-saturated robes). She has heard mystical syllables, kree, Skrull-translated tongues, and several Earth languages spoken around her on a casual basis. She has been in rooms with Tony Stark armor pieces, with the Iron Fist chi-glow as ambient nightlight, with a Quinjet's vibration. The *normal* sensory field of her childhood is the *exotic* sensory field of any other child's. She does not have, and will never have, a calibrated startle response to unusual stimuli at the levels that civilians use to gauge threat.

---

## 1.4 Formative Traumas

Three structural events, plus a chronic ambient condition.

**Trauma 1 — The *Secret Invasion* abduction (2008).** In infancy, Dani is taken from the household by "Jarvis" — who is revealed, in the cascade of *Secret Invasion*, to have been a Skrull infiltrator for an unknown but non-trivial duration. The trusted elder, the man who had held her, who Jessica had handed her child to, was an alien impostor. The recovery operation is one of the emotional spines of *Secret Invasion* for the Cage-Jones household — Jessica's panic and Luke's controlled fury are textually rendered as the rawest the characters get on page across the run. **The infant Dani herself almost certainly has no episodic memory of this.** Its formative effect on her is *transmitted* — through the recalibrated parental vigilance that ensues. From that day forward, Jessica's threat-modeling for child-care substitution is permanently revised upward, and the household's trust-extension protocols become combat-grade. The child grows up inside the *consequence* of the abduction rather than its memory: a household in which every caregiver is verified, every routine is varied, and the parent who hands the child off does not break eye contact with her until the handoff is complete.

**Trauma 2 — Civil War period parental conflict and underground living (2006–2007 publication).** With Luke as a leading anti-SHRA underground Avenger and the household subject to federal monitoring, raids, and the structural possibility of paternal arrest, Dani's infancy is conducted partially in safe-houses, partially in concealment, with intermittent paternal availability and chronic maternal threat-assessment. Iron Man's SHIELD-affiliated personnel are, at moments, *coming for her father*. The infant does not understand this. The household *operates* around it. The autonomic environment is permanent low-grade emergency.

**Trauma 3 — Repeated invasion-scale civilizational events as ambient household business.** *Secret Invasion*, *Dark Reign*, *Siege*, *Fear Itself*, *Avengers vs. X-Men*, *Infinity*, *Original Sin*, *Secret Wars* — across the publication window covering Dani's first several years, civilization-scale combat is the ambient business of both parents. She is, repeatedly, the operative reason missions are accepted (the world she will inhabit) and refused (the parent she cannot be without). She is the moral pivot of every household combat decision.

**Chronic ambient condition — the household's trauma legacy.** Both parents are survivors with active, ongoing sequelae. Jessica's Killgrave year is referenced in the household across *Alias* through the *Jessica Jones* (2016) run as a permanent fact; certain words, certain colors (purple, explicitly), certain interpersonal dynamics trigger her measurably. Luke's incarceration and the Burstein Process — the experience of being non-consensually altered in his own body — is a quieter substrate but a real one. Dani grows up in a household where the two adults she loves most are, every day, *managing* what was done to them. She learns, pre-verbally, that *being okay* is a practice rather than a state.

---

## 1.5 Formative Bonds

**With Luke.** The primary regulating bond. Tactile, slow, low-pitched, reliably present-when-present. Luke's body is, to her, the *unmovable thing in the room* — literally; she is held by a man whose skin is bulletproof and whose arms do not tire. The somatic memory of this is foundational: *I have been carried by something that nothing can break*. Across her later development this becomes the introjected sense of *containment as the form of love*. When she later, as an adult, takes up a shield, she is choosing a tool whose function is *containment of harm directed at others* — the form her father's body took in her infancy.

**With Jessica.** The primary truth-telling bond. Jessica is the parent who does not pretend. She does not soften reality, does not perform parental serenity, does not manage Dani's affect by misrepresenting her own. This is, for a child, an enormous gift: *the world is as it appears, and the appearance is being narrated by someone who will not lie to me*. Jessica is also the parent who *leaves and returns* — her PI work and her Avengers work pull her out of the home with greater unpredictability than Luke's. The bond is intense at point-of-contact and is structured around reunion rather than continuous presence. This is the substrate for Dani's later capacity to *love at distance* — to maintain attachment across operational separation, a critical competency for any field operator.

**With Danny Rand.** Uncle-of-record. Source of laughter in the household. Danny's distinctive blend of mystical seriousness and dorky earnestness gives Dani an adult model that is *neither parent* — a third register of male presence, more playful than Luke, more present than Jessica's male associates. The namesake-bond matters; she carries his name and she will carry it into adulthood.

**With Squirrel Girl.** The most evidence-rich peripheral bond. Doreen is on record as not merely competent but warmly invested. The nanny-relationship is genuinely affectionate. Dani is exposed, in this bond, to an *adult who is unconditionally cheerful* — a register her parents do not natively provide. Doreen's relentless optimism is, developmentally, a counterweight to the household's baseline grim competence.

**With the rotating Avengers.** Held by Wolverine (silently), Spider-Man (chattily), Carol Danvers (carefully), Doctor Strange (formally), Jarvis (tenderly). The aggregate signal: *adults are mostly kind, mostly competent, mostly unable to be present continuously, and almost always armed*.

**With other children.** Notably few on record. The Cage-Jones household is not socially integrated into a civilian peer-parent network in the way most child-rearing households are. Dani's age-peer exposure is sparse, intermittent, and skewed toward the children of other powered individuals (when present at all). This is a real developmental constraint and contributes, plausibly, to the later adult competency-pattern: *equal-to-equal lateral peer bonds form most easily for her in operational/team contexts* (where the structure mimics her family of origin) and less easily in casual social registers.

---

## 1.6 Earliest Behavioral Patterns

Reconstructed from the on-panel evidence across *The Pulse*, *New Avengers*, *Avengers: The Initiative*, *Power Man and Iron Fist*, *Jessica Jones* (2016), and family-unit appearances.

**Composure around violent strangers.** Repeatedly noted across the Walker *Power Man and Iron Fist* run. Costumed antagonists enter the apartment; Dani does not cry. She watches. This is not pathological flat affect — she cries when she is hungry, when she is hurt, when she is tired, on appropriate civilian cues — but the *threat cue* does not produce distress. She has been *habituated* to costumed bodies in proximity to her own from infancy. The startle-threshold for the *uncanny adult* is set, in her, at civilian-impossible levels.

**Early verbal acquisition.** Walker's run depicts a Dani who is verbal early, sentence-constructing early, and notably *deadpan* in her early utterances. The household register — Jessica's dry sarcasm, Luke's clipped pragmatism — is reproduced in her speech patterns within the first sentence-forming year. She inherits the family voice.

**Tolerance for hand-off.** Dani transitions between caregivers without protest in nearly every depicted handoff. This is a *learned* behavior. A child who has, by toddlerhood, been held by twenty different adults in routine rotation does not experience handoff as separation. This is adaptive in her environment, and in a more conventional child-rearing context would be read as evidence of either secure attachment or precocious self-regulation; in her context, both readings hold simultaneously.

**Object-directedness toward parents' tools.** On record: interest in her father's clothes, in her mother's notebooks, in Iron Fist's costume elements. The child reaches for the *implements of the parents' work*, a normal developmental pattern given how saturated her sensory environment is with those implements. The toys in her crib include, at various points, plush items modeled on Avengers iconography. The Avengers symbol is, to her, a *household decorative pattern* before it is a civic symbol.

**Sleep pattern.** Strong sleeper from infancy on record. This is a significant somatic datum. The child of an environment this stimulating could plausibly present with sleep disturbance; she does not. The autonomic baseline is *more regulated than the environment would predict*, suggesting either constitutional resilience (a genetic-inheritance read), the protective effect of two highly-present parents, or both.

**Absence of injury.** Across years of publication, no recorded injury or illness of consequence. Some of this is plot — comics under-record domestic medical events — but some is signal: she is, even pre-power-manifestation, a robust child, and she is also a child whose environment is *exhaustively* protective of her body. Her parents' chosen safety margin is *redundancy*.

**Earliest moral-affective pattern.** The Walker run shows a Dani who, asked simple moral questions ("is that nice?" "is he a bad man?") answers with directness. She has the household's clear-line moral wiring early. There is no precocity-fantasy in the books — she is a small child rendered as a small child — but the *value-system* she is being calibrated to is unambiguous: *we protect people, we do not lie about what is happening, we show up*. By age three on page, she has the rudiments of this and parrots it back in toddler register.

---

## 1.7 Synthesis of the Ground Floor

The child constructed in this period is, structurally:

- **Securely attached** to two demonstratively loving primary caregivers who model complementary regulation styles (Luke's slow containment, Jessica's truthful sharpness).
- **Network-bonded** to an unusually large rotating cast of competent secondary attachment figures, producing a baseline expectation that *safety is a collective achievement and family is constructed*.
- **Habituated** to uncanny stimuli, costumed bodies, multi-species presence, and ambient civilizational crisis to a degree that permanently resets her threat-response thresholds upward — she will, lifelong, fail to register as alarming the things that alarm everyone else.
- **Calibrated** to a moral grammar — *protect the people inside the perimeter, do not lie about reality, show up* — that is the explicit operating code of both parents and is being transmitted


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: Danielle Cage. Period covered: approximately ages 8 through 24 — the bridge years between the on-panel child of the present-day record and the on-panel adult of the displaced 20XX timeline. This section reconstructs, from canonical anchors plus forensically defensible inference, the formation arc that produced the Captain America of her era.*

---

## 6.1 The Hinge Years (approx. ages 6–10): Settling Into the Cage-Jones Operational Household

By the time Dani enters middle childhood, the household has stabilized into a distinct operational rhythm that will, in retrospect, look like the chassis on which the adult was built. Luke and Jessica are no longer in the acute-emergency posture of *Secret Invasion* and *Siege*; they have transitioned into a *sustained-readiness* posture — the household equivalent of a fire station. The Harlem residence (the brownstone-and-office configuration consistent with the Bendis-era *Jessica Jones* and Walker-era *Power Man and Iron Fist* establishing shots) functions as: (a) family home, (b) Jessica's PI practice, (c) Luke's de facto Heroes-for-Hire coordination point, and (d) Avengers-adjacent safehouse and meeting space. Dani grows up inside a building whose doorbell rings for clients, for teammates, for the wounded, and for groceries — with the child expected to read the difference by the third ring.

Behavioral micro-patterns from this period that will persist into adulthood:

- **Threshold awareness.** She is trained, implicitly, to clock who enters the house. By age seven she can name the gait of a friendly on the stair. Jessica, herself a hypervigilant survivor, does not call this training — she calls it manners. It is both.
- **Composure around adult distress.** She watches her mother debrief clients in tears, watches her father walk in bleeding through a shirt, watches Danny Rand sit at the kitchen table looking hollowed-out after a Kun-Lun summons. She learns that adults are sometimes broken and that the response is tea, a chair, and quiet. This is the proto-form of her adult bedside manner with traumatized civilians and rattled teammates.
- **Multiple-trust architecture.** The rotating caregiving network of the infancy period (Squirrel Skip, Jarvis, Drew, Iron Fist, occasionally Misty Knight) does not contract as she grows; it broadens. Birthday parties are odd. Her godparents-in-practice include people with kill counts. She learns early that love and danger are not opposites and that the people who can hurt you most are also the people most likely to die for you.
- **Code-switching across worlds.** She attends school (a Harlem public elementary, per the unspoken political commitments of both parents — neither Luke nor Jessica would tolerate Avengers-funded private placement). At school she is *Dani Cage*, a Black girl with a loud mom and a famous dad whose powers are vaguely understood and never demonstrated on premises. At home she is the daughter of the house. The two registers do not bleed into each other. The capacity to maintain two clean self-presentations across radically different audiences is laid down here and will, later, allow her to wear the Captain America cowl without losing the person under it.

The defining negative space of this period: **she is never told she will be a hero.** Both parents are explicit, in the canonical run, about wanting her to have the option of an ordinary life. Luke especially treats his powers as a fact about his body rather than a vocation she should inherit. Jessica treats vigilantism as a thing she does because no one else competent will. Neither romanticizes the work in front of the child. This is one of the most consequential parenting choices in her formation: when she later takes the shield, she takes it *uncoerced by family expectation*. The choice is hers in a way it would not have been in a household that called her destiny by name.

## 6.2 Power Onset (approx. ages 11–13): The Body Reports In

The canon does not stage Dani's powers-emergence as a single scene. The adult appearances confirm she inherited both parental lines; the developmental record must be reconstructed.

The most likely trajectory, given the parental phenotypes:

- **Subdermal durability** likely manifests first and quietly — bruises that do not form, scraped knees that heal in hours, a tumble down stairs at age nine that should have broken a wrist and did not. Luke recognizes the signs because he lives in the same body. Jessica notes them with the dread of someone who hoped, against her own evidence, that the child would be spared the conscription of an unusual physiology.
- **Strength** ramps in over the early-pubertal window. A doorknob comes off in her hand. A school desk moves when she pushes back from it. She learns to *modulate before she learns to test* — the inverse of the typical powered-adolescent trajectory, and a direct downstream consequence of being raised by two adults who have themselves had to learn that modulation the hard way. Luke teaches her to hold an egg, to shake a hand, to open a door. Jessica teaches her to fly low and land softly, in a vacant lot at night, no audience. (Jessica's own flight is canonically inconsistent and effortful; she is a competent rather than confident flyer, and she passes on the *competent-flyer* posture rather than the *confident-flyer* posture. Dani as an adult does not appear to fly as a primary mode of locomotion — consistent with a mother who taught her flight as an emergency tool, not a default.)
- **Pain-anticipation muting.** This is the developmentally subtlest inheritance and the one with the longest tail. By age twelve she has internalized that her body does not punish her for risk in the way other children's bodies do. The interoceptive feedback loop that brakes ordinary adolescent recklessness is, in her, dampened. Both parents notice. Both parents compensate with explicit cognitive rules: *we do not jump from the roof because we can. we do not because we model for civilians what is possible and we will not teach a child watching from a window that jumping is safe*. The rules-by-principle rather than rules-by-pain pattern she will operate under as an adult begins here.

The household response to power onset is, characteristically, *de-spectacularized*. There is no ceremony, no costume, no announcement. Jessica's blunt-affect frame — *this is a thing about you now, we deal with it* — combined with Luke's deliberate calm produces a child who experiences her own powers as a matter of fact rather than a matter of identity. This is psychologically protective and will, later, allow her to take on the most identity-laden costume in the catalog without confusing the costume for the self.

## 6.3 Adolescence (approx. ages 13–17): The Civilian Years That Weren't Quite

Two countervailing pressures define her teens.

**Pressure One: the parental project of normalcy.** Luke and Jessica conspicuously do not enroll her in any Avengers-academy structure. The Initiative-era and Champions-era youth-hero pipelines are available; both parents decline, repeatedly, on her behalf and then, as she ages into consent, in active conversation with her. The argument, reconstructed from the parental value-sets visible across canon: *the work finds you; you do not need to find it early; childhood is not a runway*. She attends a public high school. She plays a sport — basketball is the most defensible inference given her father's frame, her mother's reflexes, and the cultural geography of her upbringing; she plays it without using her strength, which becomes its own discipline. She has friends who do not know what her parents do beyond the broad-strokes celebrity of the Cage name. She dates, probably awkwardly, probably without ever bringing anyone home twice.

**Pressure Two: the gravitational pull of the household's actual business.** She cannot un-know what she has grown up inside. She reads her mother's case files without permission. She listens to her father's after-action calls through the heating vent. By fifteen she has, almost certainly, made unsanctioned observational excursions — tailing a person of interest from her mother's caseload to a subway stop, surveilling a building her father mentioned over dinner, returning home before either parent notices. The PI-trained pattern-matching she will display as an adult is not native talent alone; it is a teenager who taught herself her mother's craft in secret because no one would teach her openly.

The compromise her parents eventually negotiate, in the late-teen window, is the household-defining one: **she may train; she may not deploy.** Luke takes her through bodywork — the standing, the breathing, the absorption of a strike, the deliberate non-escalation that defines his fighting style. Jessica takes her through case discipline — chain of evidence, surveillance ethics, when to call a cop and when not to. Iron Fist, when in town, almost certainly gives her formal striking instruction; the structure and economy of motion she will later display under the shield is more Rand-discipline than Cage-brawling, suggesting deliberate transmission. Misty Knight likely contributes firearms familiarity and tactical movement — Dani as an adult handles the shield with the situational awareness of someone trained on a long gun, scanning sectors rather than tracking single threats.

She is, by seventeen, a trained operator with no operational history. This is unusual and protective. She has the skills without the trauma backlog that ordinarily comes with acquiring them in the field.

## 6.4 The Inflection (approx. ages 17–19): First Independent Choices

The earliest defining choices visible in her later self-presentation:

- **The choice to stay in Harlem for college.** The adult subject, when she speaks of her origin, places her family geographically — not in Avengers Tower, not on a coast, in *Harlem*. The continuity of place is a choice she made when she could have left. A child reared by two people who escaped places that tried to break them, choosing to stay in the place that did not, is making a statement of inheritance.
- **The choice to study something useful and unglamorous.** No canonical confirmation, but the operational vocabulary the adult uses — chain-of-command terminology, logistical fluency, a comfort with public-service registers — is consistent with training in something like public policy, emergency management, or pre-law. Not a hero degree. A *governance* degree. This matters: she will take the shield as a person trained to think about institutions rather than as a person trained to punch through them.
- **The choice to keep her name.** Many children of powered parents adopt code names early. She does not. *Danielle Cage* is the operational name she carries into adulthood and onto the cowl. (When she puts on the shield, she does not rename herself; she becomes *Captain America* on duty and *Danielle Cage* off, in the older Steve Rogers model rather than the legacy-rename model.) The refusal to fragment her identity behind an alias is the same psychological move her father made — *Carl Lucas became Luke Cage and stayed Luke Cage; he did not become a mask*. She inherits the *no-mask* posture even when she will eventually wear one.

## 6.5 The Catastrophe (approx. ages 19–22): The Loss That Forged the Adult

The displaced-future canon establishes, in past-tense, that adult-Dani's parents are dead or absent and that the world she came up in has shifted enough that the Captain America mantle has become available to her. The specifics are not on-panel. The forensically defensible reconstruction:

The catastrophe is not a single event. It is, almost certainly, a *cascade*. One parent first, then — in the destabilization that follows — the second. Luke first is the more probable order, given Jessica's documented operational capacity to function under loss (her entire pre-Cage life was that) and given that the adult subject's regulatory posture resembles a Jessica who has had time to teach a daughter how to grieve before that mother, too, was taken. Luke's loss would be a wound; Jessica's loss would be the closing of the family unit. The interval between is the formative period — months, perhaps a year or two, in which a young woman in her early twenties lives alone with a mother who is herself grieving, and learns to hold a household together for someone who taught her how.

Steve Rogers' death or retirement, in this timeline, plausibly bracketed or followed the parental losses. The chronology that produces the cleanest adult presentation: Luke dies; Jessica enters a terminal grieving arc and dies (operationally, in the field, or by attrition — the canon is silent and the silence is doing work); Steve, who in this displaced timeline is by this point an aging figure, either falls in a comparable action or transitions out and asks her to take the shield. The triple loss — biological father, biological mother, symbolic father — concentrated within a short window in her early twenties, is the forge. She emerges from it with the shield, the cowl, and the regulatory composure that the present-day Avengers later find unnerving.

What she does *not* do in this window is more diagnostic than what she does:

- **She does not break.** No reported decompensation, no withdrawal, no substance episode, no acting-out. The defenses hold.
- **She does not isolate.** She attaches to surviving network members — Danny Rand if alive, Misty if alive, the broader Avengers-residue community — and reconstitutes a family-by-team within the working period of acute grief. This is the same multiple-trust architecture from childhood, deployed under load.
- **She does not refuse the shield.** Offered the most symbolically freighted object in the catalog at the worst moment of her life, she takes it. The introjective grief solution begins in the same gesture. She is, in that moment, choosing to become the principle that survives the people. The choice is not coerced and not romantic; it is, by her own internal logic, the only available container for what she is carrying.

## 6.6 The First Year Under the Shield (approx. ages 22–23): Public Emergence

The first operational year as Captain America is, by the structure of her later presentation, the period in which the *Cap-on-duty / Dani-off-duty* split is consolidated. She learns, in this year:

- **That the cowl is a tool of crowd management.** Civilians respond to the silhouette before they respond to the person. She uses this. She develops the steady, low, projected voice that the adult appearances confirm — a voice trained for crowd-pacification, not for camera-charisma.
- **That the shield is a conversation.** Both offensive and defensive, both threat and pledge. Her shield-work in the adult appearances reads as *defensive-priority* — she shields others before she throws — and this is plausibly the doctrine she developed in the first year, downstream from a father whose entire fighting style was *absorb so others do not have to*.
- **That she will be compared and that she must not compete.** Steve Rogers' shadow is the largest in the symbolic field she has entered. She makes the early, consequential decision to *not perform Steve* — to carry the object without imitating the man. This is visible in her later interactions: she does not invoke him as an authority, does not quote him, does not stage herself in his iconography. She is *the next Captain America*, not *the Steve Rogers tribute act*. This is the same move her father made with the Hero for Hire tradition — inherit the function, refuse the cosplay.
- **That her parents' deaths must be spoken about cleanly or not at all.** She develops the past-tense composure that present-day observers will later find either admirable or alarming. The composure is constructed in this year, in the first dozen times she is asked, by strangers and by press, *who her parents were*.

## 6.7 The Displacement (approx. age 23–24): Arrival in the Present

The time-displacement event that delivers her to the present-day Marvel timeline (per *U.S.Avengers*) places her in a world that contains her living parents. The canon understates the affective load; the file does not.

The behavioral micro-record of the displacement period, reconstructed from the on-panel adult presentation:

- **Initial contact protocols.** She does not, on arrival, seek out present-day Luke and Jessica. She integrates with the team first. This is *not* avoidance in the pathological sense; it is operational sequencing — she stabilizes her professional footing before exposing herself to the most affectively dangerous encounter available in the present.
- **Eventual contact.** When the contact happens, she manages it with the past-tense composure she developed in the first year under the shield, now deployed under the strangest possible conditions — speaking to people in the room who are, in her experience, dead. She does not weep. She does not embrace beyond the conventional. She does not call them *Mom* and *Dad*. The boundary work is enormous and almost entirely invisible. The cost is paid somewhere off-panel, in a hotel room or a quinjet bunk, in private.
- **Integration with the team-as-family.** Da Costa's U.S.Avengers becomes, within the displacement window, her present-tense family-by-team. The pattern from childhood — *safety is a team enterprise* — repeats under the most adverse conditions imaginable. She is, again, the child who learned that the people in the perimeter are the people who count, and the perimeter is wherever she has set it today.

## 6.8 Fallout — What the Formation Produced

The Captain America visible in the adult appearances is the readable output of this formation arc, point for point:

- **The composure** is the household composure of two trauma-survivors raised her in, scaled up by repeated loss.
- **The defensive-priority combat doctrine** is her father's posture, weaponized with her mother's tactical sense and Rand's economy of motion.
- **The refusal to perform Steve** is her father's refusal to perform the legacy versions of his own name.
- **The team-as-family operational unit** is the multiple-trust architecture of her childhood, load-tested by catastrophe.
- **The past-tense composure about her parents** is the first-year-under-the-shield construction, hardened by repetition.
- **The unprocessed zone** — the place where the cost is being paid — is the same zone present in the formation: *she was never required to grieve out loud, because the role absorbed it*. The role is, structurally, still absorbing it.

She emerges from the formation period as exactly what her parents managed to build before the world took them — and as the proof, carried forward in a body that does not break, that what they built was load-bearing under the worst weight the future could put on it.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

*The long middle of Danielle Cage's existence is, by necessity, a stitched record. The published canon gives us the granular childhood (Bendis-era infancy through Walker-era toddlerhood) and the finished adult product (Remender → Ewing's Captain America of 20XX). The middle — the years between five-or-six and the moment she lifts the shield — is the negative space the entire arc requires. A total-surveillance file does not skip this period; it reconstructs it from the bracketing data, from the household it was lived inside, and from the woman who emerged from it. What follows is that reconstruction, anchored at every load-bearing point to canon, with the interstitial pattern-work flagged as such.*

---

## 1. The Late-Toddler Through Early-Latency Window (Approx. Subjective Ages 4–8)

### 1.1 The Household at Operating Tempo

By this period the Cage-Jones household has stabilized into its mature pattern, the one *Power Man and Iron Fist* (Walker, 2016) and the second Bendis *Jessica Jones* (2016) lock in. The family is no longer pre-figuring out what it is; it is running. Functionally this means:

- **Two working vigilante parents** with overlapping but non-identical operational tempos. Luke runs the Heroes for Hire revival with Danny Rand — sporadic, episodic, often street-level Harlem work that keeps him in-borough. Jessica runs Alias Investigations out of the apartment, which means her *physical* presence is higher but her *attentional* presence is lower; she is on cases, on the phone, on a laptop, mid-thought.
- **Harlem as deliberate geography.** The family does not move uptown, downtown, or out. The choice is consistent across the run and is repeatedly verbalized by both parents: Harlem is where Luke is *legible* — to neighbors, to the corner, to the people he protects. Dani's developmental geography is therefore narrow and dense: the same blocks, the same bodega, the same stoops, the same handful of caregivers cycling through. This is the opposite of the cosmopolitan-tower upbringing she could have had via Avengers infrastructure; it is a deliberate, principled localism.
- **The rotating extended caregiving network**, established in the *New Avengers* vol. 2 nursery period, persists in attenuated form: Squirrel Girl off-and-on, Jessica Drew episodically, Misty Knight as the most reliable non-parent, Danny Rand as quasi-uncle, Jarvis as occasional and complicated (the Skrull echo never fully discharges from Jessica's nervous system, and a surveillance reading of the household would note Jarvis-visits as marked-anxious events for Jessica even years out).

### 1.2 Behavioral Micro-Pattern: The Composed Child

The Walker-era depictions establish a child who is verbal early, observant, and conspicuously unstartled by violent strangers in the apartment. A surveillance read extrapolates this forward across the early-latency window with high confidence:

- **No startle response to sudden noise in domestic space.** A child raised inside a household where the front door is regularly broken — Power Man's apartment door is a running visual gag in his solo title — habituates. The exteroceptive baseline shifts. Sirens, shouting in the hall, the apartment shaking from a fight one floor down: ambient, not alarming.
- **Startle response preserved, however, for *parental affect change*.** This is the inverse pattern and the meaningful one. She does not react to a stranger smashing through the window; she reacts to her mother going quiet mid-sentence. The discriminator she has trained is not threat-in-the-environment but threat-as-registered-by-trusted-adults. This is the somatic substrate of every later behavior in the adult record: she reads rooms through her people, not through her own threat-sense.
- **Early bilingual code-switching of register.** Her father speaks to her in one cadence (low, slow, declarative, the deliberate calm that is Luke's defining vocal pattern); her mother in another (clipped, ironic, profane-with-suppression-around-the-child, the Jessica register). By age five she is fluent in both and uses each appropriately to the parent. This is the prototype of the later adult code-switching between command voice, teammate voice, and the rarer civilian voice.

### 1.3 The Pedagogy Of Two Survivors

Both parents are teaching her, deliberately, and they are teaching her different curricula:

- **Luke's curriculum is *bearing*.** How to hold your body in a room. How to walk past trouble without performing past-it. How to be physically large in a way that does not threaten the people who need you to be large. How to take a punch (eventually — this is later) without retaliating from anger. The Burstein-era ethic, filtered through a decade of post-prison work: *the worst thing a strong man can do is be afraid, because frightened strong men do harm*.
- **Jessica's curriculum is *seeing*.** How to read a room. How to tell when an adult is lying. How to ask the question that the person does not want asked. How to be unimpressed by power. Jessica's PI training is, in its core, the discipline of refusing to be deceived, and she transmits this not as a lesson but as a constant atmospheric practice — Dani grows up watching her mother *not believe* people, with affection, and learns by observation that love and disbelief are compatible.
- **What neither parent teaches, conspicuously.** The mythos. Neither Luke nor Jessica is a flag-waver. Neither talks about the Avengers as a sacred institution. Neither mythologizes Captain America to her — which is, in retrospect, one of the most analytically important negative findings in the entire dossier. The adult subject takes up Rogers's shield without having been raised to revere it. This means the shield, when she takes it, is not the fulfillment of a childhood worship; it is an *operational* choice made by an adult who saw something needed carrying. The distinction matters enormously for understanding her relationship to the role.

### 1.4 The First Conscious Losses

The middle period necessarily contains the first deaths Dani registers as deaths rather than as absences. Given household exposure, these are statistically certain even within the conservative canon — the Cage-Jones extended professional network sustains casualties across every major event of the period. The behavioral marker to note is the family's *response* to these losses, which becomes the template:

- Luke does not weep in front of her but does not hide that he has wept.
- Jessica explains, in plain language, what has happened and does not euphemize.
- The funeral is attended; the work resumes the next morning.

The pedagogy here is the most consequential of her childhood: *grief is real, grief is named, grief does not stop the work*. This is the exact algorithm the adult subject runs when discussing her dead parents in clean past-tense. She did not invent it; she inherited it.

---

## 2. Mid-Latency Through Pre-Adolescence (Approx. Subjective Ages 8–12)

### 2.1 The Powers Question

Canon is deliberately silent on when Dani's inherited physiology activates. Child-Dani is "unusually robust." Adult-Dani exceeds Luke's strength baseline. Somewhere in this window — and the developmentally plausible answer is *gradual onset through mid-latency with full manifestation around puberty* — she begins to register as different from her peers.

The behavioral consequences in the middle period are predictable and granular:

- **First broken object.** A door handle, a tabletop, a toy. The age at which a child first realizes their hands can do damage they did not intend is, in a normal child, never; in this one, it is probably eight or nine. The parents' response is critical and, given their identities, almost certainly excellent: Luke has lived inside an unbreakable body his entire adult life and knows the entire phenomenology of accidentally damaging the world. He teaches her the modulation as something normal, not something shameful. This is the origin of her adult somatic poise.
- **First test of skin.** A scraped knee that does not scrape. A kitchen burn that does not burn. The cognitive recalibration — *my body is not going to register the things other bodies register* — is processed in a household where one parent has lived it for years. She has a guide.
- **First withheld strike.** Almost certainly with another child. A schoolyard incident, a flare of temper, a swing that connects harder than intended. The lesson absorbed: she cannot fight the way other children fight. The lesson she internalizes more deeply: *she cannot afford to lose her temper*. This is, plausibly, the origin point of her adult low-expressed-emotion baseline. It is not constitutional; it is trained. The training began when she realized her body was a liability to anyone she was angry with.

### 2.2 The Schooling Decision

A surveillance file cares about this because schooling shapes peer-attachment, civic-formation, and routine. The parents' choice is constrained: a powered child of two public superhumans, in Harlem, with a family threat profile that has included Skrulls, the Hood, the Purple Man, and assorted Civil-War-era federal interest. The plausible solution is a hybrid — neighborhood public school for as long as it is safe, with a switch to a more controlled environment (a tutored arrangement, a vetted private school, eventually quite possibly Avengers-network mentorship for the powered-curriculum component) when it ceases to be.

The behavioral residue: she is comfortable with normal kids and comfortable with extraordinary ones, and she does not mythologize either group. She is unimpressed by superhumans and unpatronizing toward civilians. The flatness of her status-affect, visible in *U.S.Avengers* in how she treats Roberto Da Costa (a billionaire mutant industrialist) exactly the same as she treats Squirrel Girl (her former nanny, now teammate), is the trace of this dual-track formation.

### 2.3 The Purple Man Period As Permanent Household Variable

No middle-period account of this household is complete without naming the load-bearing absence at its center: Killgrave. The Purple Man returns in *Jessica Jones* (2016) and has returned, in various forms, across the entire Bendis run. He is the threat that does not resolve. He is the threat that Jessica cannot fully metabolize. He is the one variable that turns Jessica from a competent operator into a person who has to *stop and re-regulate*.

For a child in the household across years, this is not an event; this is a weather system. Dani learns, without anyone telling her, the following:

- There is a category of threat that the adults treat differently.
- Her mother becomes a different person when this category is invoked.
- Certain phone calls, certain news items, certain names cause the apartment temperature to drop.

She does not need to be told who Killgrave is to learn the contour of his shadow. By pre-adolescence she has filed: *some harms are not the kind you punch.* This is, again, a load-bearing piece of her adult psychology — her instinctive understanding that some battles are interior and ongoing and never quite won, that competence does not mean closure, and that her mother's strength is not the absence of damage but the daily management of it.

### 2.4 The Marriage She Watches

The middle period is the years she actually *sees* the marriage, with adult eyes forming. The Cage-Jones bond as depicted in canon — across Bendis, Walker, and the various event books — is one of the more functional partnerships in the line, and notably one that is *worked at* rather than assumed. They fight. They reconcile. They have separated and reunited (the brief *Pulse*/*New Avengers* tensions, the operational separations during *Civil War II*, the recurring debates about retirement). They are demonstrative — physical affection, verbal warmth, casual touch — to a degree that is genuinely uncommon in the Marvel marriage corpus.

What Dani takes from observing this across years:

- Partnership is collaboration under pressure, not freedom from pressure.
- Conflict is normal and survivable.
- Affection is shown, not assumed.
- The other person is allowed to be wrong.

Her adult deficit, flagged in the personality section, is intimacy that is *not* mission-mediated. This is not because she lacks a model of love; it is because the only model she has is love-under-fire. Her parents' bond was forged in mutual rescue, sustained through mutual operation, and stress-tested by recurring catastrophe. She has no template for the bond that exists across a quiet Tuesday afternoon when no one needs saving. The deficit is not absence; it is *specificity*.

---

## 3. Adolescence (Approx. Subjective Ages 12–17)

### 3.1 The Power Settles

By mid-adolescence the inherited physiology is fully online. Strength at or exceeding Luke's baseline. Durability comparable to or exceeding his. Whether she manifests any of the Kree-derivative aspects of Jessica's profile (the flight, in particular) is unclear from the adult record — adult-Dani is not depicted in flight, suggesting either non-inheritance or non-development. The likeliest configuration: full Burstein-spectrum inheritance from the paternal line, partial Kree-derivative inheritance from the maternal (the strength yes, the flight no or attenuated).

The somatic consequences of adolescence in this body are particular. The normal adolescent experience of a body that is changing faster than the self can track is amplified into a body that is also *more dangerous than the self can track*. The discipline she develops here — the constant, micro-second-by-micro-second modulation of force — becomes the substrate of her adult composure. People read her as calm; she is, in fact, *continuously throttling*. The throttling is so habitual by adulthood that it presents as serenity.

### 3.2 Training

The training curriculum in this window is the densest data the bracketing record implies. A powered teenage daughter of two combat-experienced parents, with an extended network including Iron Fist (the most accomplished living martial artist of his generation), Misty Knight (tactical and small-unit specialist), Jessica Drew (espionage and infiltration), and intermittent access to broader Avengers and S.H.I.E.L.D.-adjacent instruction — this is an unparalleled training environment. The adult subject's competence, depicted in *U.S.Avengers* as immediately compatible with high-end superhuman team operations, requires this kind of curriculum.

What she is most plausibly trained in, by whom:

- **Striking, grappling, modulated force.** Luke, primarily; Iron Fist for the technical layer.
- **Tactical movement, small-team coordination, the use of the environment.** Misty.
- **Infiltration, deception, reading marks, working under cover.** Jessica Drew, with Jessica Jones contributing the investigative-interrogation layer.
- **Operational ethics — when to act, when to stand down, what a hero will and will not do.** Luke, primarily; this is his core teaching.
- **The reading of institutions — when a badge is real, when a warrant is legitimate, when a federal agent is acting in bad faith.** Jessica. This is the curriculum of a woman who has had her trust in institutions broken specifically and repeatedly.

She is not, in this period, being trained to be Captain America. She is being trained to be *capable*. The specific role comes later and is, again, her own adult choice.

### 3.3 The First Real Field Operation

Statistically certain across adolescence: an event in which she is on the wrong end of someone's plan and her parents cannot fully shield her. This is the moment the *security envelope* of childhood thins, and her own competence has to substitute. The most behaviorally consequential micro-moment in this period is, almost certainly, the first time she takes a hit that should have killed someone and stands up to keep fighting. The cognitive realization — *I do not break* — is not joyful. It is sobering. The kids who learn this lesson learn it as license; she learns it as obligation. The difference is, again, the household.

### 3.4 The Adolescent Rupture (And Its Absence)

Most adolescents rupture with their parents. The data on whether Dani did is unrecoverable, but the adult evidence suggests the rupture was, if it occurred, brief and incomplete. The adult subject's stance toward her parents is *integrated* — composed grief, identification with their values, no detectable resentment. This is not the affective profile of someone who left her parents' house in a slam-door fury that she never got to repair. It is the profile of someone whose adolescence with her parents resolved, and whose loss of them came later, in adulthood, with the relationship in good standing.

This is, in surveillance terms, *fortunate* and not accidental. Luke and Jessica are, by parent-quality measures, unusually good — present, fallible, honest, demonstrative. The conditions for a survivable adolescence were met.

---

## 4. The Hinge Period — Early Adulthood Before The Loss (Approx. Subjective Ages 17–early 20s)

### 4.1 Independent Operation

In the years before the catastrophic loss that defines the adult timeline, Dani is plausibly operating independently or semi-independently. The most likely vector, given the household network, is association with one of the family-adjacent teams — the Heroes for Hire infrastructure, an Avengers training cohort, the next-generation team-of-the-era (whatever that is in her timeline). She is *known* in the community. She has a working identity that is not yet Captain America. The shield is, in this period, still Rogers's, or someone else's.

Her operational reputation in this window is the bedrock the later mantle is granted on. People do not hand the shield to a stranger.

### 4.2 The Parents' Late Period

In the years immediately before their deaths in her timeline, Luke and Jessica are aging but still operational. The relationship to her shifts from parent-of-child to parent-of-peer — the most rewarding phase of any parent-child relationship, and the one she gets to have. This is critical for understanding the *cleanness* of her adult grief. She got the adult relationship. She got to know them as people, not just as parents. The mourning, when it comes, is for a complete relationship rather than a truncated one.

### 4.3 The Loss

The canon does not specify how Luke and Jessica die in the 20XX timeline. The behavioral evidence from the adult record permits a narrow inference: it was not slow. The adult subject does not present with the somatic markers of long caregiving (the chronic-vigilance posture, the touch-aversion, the disordered sleep patterns of someone who lived years at the bedside). She presents with the markers of *acute catastrophic loss followed by sustained mission-construction*. The plausible reading: a combat death or deaths, possibly together, in a larger event that left the world meaningfully worse and the symbolic role of Captain America meaningfully vacant.

The behavioral aftermath, reconstructed from the woman who arrives in *U.S.Avengers*:

- A short period of operational withdrawal — weeks or a few months, not years. Long enough to bury them and not function. Not long enough to atrophy.
- A re-emergence anchored in *the work that remained*. Whatever team or community she returned to, she returned with the question already answered: *the work does not stop because you are tired*. The pedagogy held.
- The taking up of the shield. Not immediately; the mantle is offered, considered, accepted. The decision is sober. She does not want it. She takes it because the alternative — the shield in a case, the symbol unworn, the role unfilled in a world that needed it — was unacceptable to the value-system her parents transmitted.

### 4.4 The First Years As Captain America

By the time she appears in the present-day Marvel record via time-displacement, she has been Captain America long enough to be *settled* into the role. She is not a rookie under the cowl. She moves with practiced authority, gives orders with calibrated weight, and treats the shield as a tool whose physics she has internalized. The plausible duration: years, not months.

These years are the years the present record cannot see. They are the years she became the woman who arrives. They contain, almost certainly:

- The first teammate she lost under her command — the pressure-point flagged in the personality assessment, almost certainly tested in this window, almost certainly survived but at cost.
- The first time she had to make a strategic decision that produced civilian casualties — the ethical wound that Captain America accumulates and that her father's pedagogy did not fully prepare her for, because Luke's ethic was *no one gets hurt on my watch* and command requires the violation of that ethic.
- The construction of her command voice, her press posture, her institutional protocols. The shield is a public office; she had to learn to hold it publicly.
- The accumulation of the chosen-family-as-team pattern that she brings, intact, to Da Costa's roster in the displaced-present.

By the end of this period she is the finished subject. Composed, competent, mission-organized, mature-defended, ethically anchored, deeply held by a network she has constructed from scratch in a world where her family of origin is gone. The middle period — the long bracketed years — is what made her. The household at the beginning of it, and the woman at the end of it, are the two endpoints between which everything else is the work of becoming.

---

*The defining middle period is the period in which the security envelope of childhood is gradually withdrawn and replaced by an internalized envelope she carries herself. The Cage-Jones household built a child who could survive becoming an orphan and still serve. The middle period is the years in which that construction was tested incrementally, each test passed, until the final test came and the construction held. Everything in the adult record — the composure, the mature defenses, the team-as-family pattern, the clean past-tense grief, the shield held without theology — is the deferred output of a curriculum delivered patiently across two decades by two people who, between them, knew exactly what it costs to survive what the world does, and intended for their daughter to be able to do it.*


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

## Preamble: What "Recent" Means for a Bifurcated Subject

Two clocks run on this file. The child-Dani exists in the present-day Marvel chronology — currently rendered as a small child in Harlem, the operational center of the Cage-Jones household, latest substantive appearances clustering around the *Devil's Reign*, *Mayor Fisk*, *Devil's Reign: Omega*, and the *Power Man: Timeless* / Heroes Reborn–adjacent material, plus the family's appearances in *Iron Fist: Heart of the Dragon*, *Cage's Iron Fist*–related team-ups, and the loose constellation of Marvel "family unit" interstitials that have followed since the conclusion of Bendis's *Jessica Jones* (2016–2018). The adult-Dani exists in the displaced 20XX timeline, last canonically active in the *U.S.Avengers* arc and its *Generations: The Americas* coda (2017), with subsequent appearances in the time-traveler-roster ensembles (the *Avengers Forever* / *Avengers Assemble* lineages of multiversal Avengers, where she has been catalogued as the Captain America of her thread and slotted into the broader Multiversal Masters of the Reality / Mephisto-era continuity). The two timelines update each other recursively: every development in the child's present revises the adult's backstory, and every published appearance of the adult constrains the child's projected trajectory.

This section maps both clocks at their current setting and the path each took to arrive there.

---

## 1. The Child Clock — Harlem, Present Day

### 1.1 Residence and Household Footprint

The Cage-Jones unit currently operates out of a Harlem brownstone apartment, the family's third or fourth residence of record since Dani's birth. The pattern of moves has been responsive rather than aspirational: a relocation after the Power Man and Iron Fist–era street-war drew unwanted attention to the Gem Theater / Pop's Barbershop axis; a defensive consolidation during the post-Hydra-occupation cleanup of Manhattan (*Secret Empire* aftermath, 2017–2018); and a quieter intra-Harlem shift during the *Devil's Reign* mayoral period when Wilson Fisk's anti-vigilante apparatus made known-superhero addresses operationally hot. The current address is not publicly disclosed in canon — itself a tell: the writers' rooms across the relevant runs have *stopped* placing the home on-panel with locational specificity, which mirrors what an actual security-conscious household would do.
