## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

## Preamble: Two Lives, One Subject

A surveillance file on Danielle Cage must be kept in two parallel ledgers, because the subject's daily existence runs on two incompatible clocks. The **child-Dani ledger** records a small person whose schedule is dictated by the operational tempo of two superhuman parents and a rotating caregiving cohort in Harlem and lower Manhattan. The **adult-Dani ledger** records a time-displaced operator living out of a go-bag on a fast-response team, whose "home" is provisional and whose week is structured by the next deployment. The throughline — the granular tells that persist from infancy into adulthood — is the more interesting record. A surveillance team would notice, by the second week, that the child and the adult **hold a spoon the same way**, **scan a room in the same sequence**, and **wake at the same time**.

---

## I. CHILD-DANI: THE HARLEM DOMESTIC RECORD (Infancy through ~age 5)

### A. The Wake Cycle and Morning Routine

Dani is, from infancy, a consistent early waker — a pattern that originates not in temperament but in environment. The Cage-Jones residence is a household where one or both parents are frequently up by 0500–0530 either coming off a night call or scanning the early news for what they will have to deal with that day. The infant calibrates to the ambient sound floor. By toddlerhood, Dani is reliably awake between 0615 and 0700, almost never later.

The morning sequence, reconstructed from background panels and continuity references across *New Avengers* (vol. 2), *Power Man and Iron Fist*, and *Jessica Jones* (2016):

- **First handler:** Luke, more often than Jessica. The division is not philosophical but pragmatic — Jessica works late on cases and is a poor early riser; Luke runs Heroes for Hire / the bar / the building out of normal business hours. Dani is in Luke's arms or on his hip for the first 30–60 minutes of nearly every weekday morning.
- **Feeding station:** the kitchen counter, not a high chair. Luke prefers to keep her physically against him while he eats and reads; she is fed off his plate from a very young age, which produces an unusually omnivorous palate (greens, eggs, hot sauce in microdoses, coffee-tasting from a spoon by age three over Jessica's objection).
- **Jessica's entry:** typically 0730–0830, in pajamas or yesterday's clothes, coffee first, child second. The handoff is wordless and routine. Dani learned early not to demand a particular parent at a particular hour; she takes who is available.
- **The dressing ritual:** Dani is dressed by whichever parent is leaving the apartment second. By age three she is choosing her own clothes and showing a marked preference for her father's color palette — yellows, mustards, browns — a tell noted by anyone who looked.

### B. The Caregiving Rotation (the "Watchers' Roster")

A surveillance file would build a roster, with frequency-of-contact metrics:

- **Squirrel Girl (Doreen Green):** highest non-parent contact during 2010–2012, scheduled days, brings textbooks and the squirrels. Dani's first reliably-recognized word, per *New Avengers* vol. 2 background dialogue, is approximately "Tippy" before "Daddy."
- **Jarvis:** trusted but, post-*Secret Invasion*, with a permanent asterisk in Jessica's internal classification system. Used for short windows only.
- **Jessica Drew:** crisis backup; Dani is comfortable with her, calls her by name early.
- **Danny Rand:** frequent during the *Power Man and Iron Fist* period; Dani is left at the Heroes for Hire office with him during day-trip cases. He reads to her from texts she cannot possibly understand and she sits through it.
- **Misty Knight and Colleen Wing:** secondary rotation; Misty in particular is one of the few people Jessica trusts with overnight care.
- **Aunt Carol (Danvers):** ceremonial more than functional, but present at milestones; the godmother role is taken seriously.

A pattern emerges: Dani's caregivers are, almost without exception, **combat-capable, female-skewed, and bonded to her parents by shared survival rather than blood**. She grows up understanding "family" as *the people who would take a bullet for you, in or out of the household*.

### C. The Apartment as Operational Space

The Cage-Jones residence is a domestic space overlaid with a security envelope. Specific habits a surveillance team would log:

- **Sightlines.** Dani is rarely placed in any room where neither parent can see her from their working position. Her play area in the living room is in line of sight from the kitchen counter (Luke's coffee station) and the desk Jessica works from.
- **The "go" bag.** A diaper bag — later, by age three, a small backpack — is by the door at all times, pre-packed: snacks, water, change of clothes, a stuffed lion she will not sleep without, a sealed envelope with cash, an emergency contact card laminated by Jessica. Refreshed weekly. Dani learns by age four to grab it on command.
- **Hand signals.** Luke and Jessica use a small set of household hand signals (originally developed for in-fight communication) that Dani absorbs by observation. By age three she knows *stay there*, *come here now*, and *quiet*. She uses them on her parents, which they find both unnerving and useful.
- **The "loud night" protocol.** If Dani wakes to combat-adjacent noise from elsewhere in the building or the street, she goes to the bathtub — drilled by Jessica, who chose the bathroom for its interior walls and lack of windows. Dani is observed enacting this drill in *Jessica Jones* background work.

### D. Meals, Naps, Bedtime

- **Lunch:** flexible, often takeout from one of two-three Harlem restaurants where the staff know the family. Dani is widely recognized at these establishments by toddlerhood; she is greeted by name.
- **Nap:** 1300–1500, non-negotiable, enforced by whoever is on duty. The nap is the load-bearing structural element of the entire household — every adult schedules around it.
- **Dinner:** the most reliably family-unit-attended meal. Both parents are physically present roughly four nights of seven. The TV is off. Phones are on the counter, not the table — a Luke rule.
- **Bath:** Luke's domain almost exclusively. He sings (off-key, country-tinged) and tells her stories that are sanitized versions of his own week.
- **Bedtime:** 1930–2000, Jessica reads. The reading material is not children's books exclusively; Jessica reads from her own books, including newspapers and case files, in a soft voice. The content does not matter — the voice does. Dani falls asleep to the rhythm of her mother's voice processing the day's evidence.

### E. The Tells

By age four, Dani exhibits a stable cluster of micro-behaviors a surveillance team would catalogue:

- **Room scan on entry.** She enters every new room with a brief, almost imperceptible head-turn — left, right, up. This is observed parental modeling (both parents do it) absorbed before language.
- **Composure around strangers, including violent ones.** She does not cry at the appearance of unknown enhanced individuals in her home. She watches them. This is statistically anomalous for a child her age.
- **Touch-seeking with her father, voice-seeking with her mother.** She climbs Luke; she calls for Jessica. The differentiation is consistent across all observed episodes.
- **Food-sharing as bonding.** She offers food off her own plate to adults she trusts. By age three this is a reliable affiliation marker.
- **The stuffed lion.** Carried, dropped, retrieved, demanded. By age five it is matted and one ear is sewn back on by Luke. It travels.

---

## II. THE LATENT YEARS

The canon does not document Dani's middle childhood, adolescence, or early adulthood in detail; the timeline ruptures and reconvenes with her as a young adult in costume. A surveillance file would mark this as the **dark window** — the period during which the foundational patterns established above were either preserved, eroded, or weaponized by what happened to her family. Inference from the adult subject suggests:

- The room-scan persisted and sharpened.
- The food-sharing affiliation marker survived (she shares rations with teammates with notable frequency).
- The lion did not survive, or did, and is in a footlocker somewhere she does not open.
- The bathtub protocol became something more elaborate and operational.
- The morning hand-off to a present parent was replaced by waking alone, which she did not learn to enjoy.

---

## III. ADULT-DANI: THE DEPLOYED-OPERATOR RECORD

The adult subject lives, during her *U.S.Avengers* period and after, as a time-displaced asset attached to a fast-response team operating out of a mobile command structure. "Home" is a bunk, a footlocker, and a hook for the shield. The daily record reads less like a domestic life and more like a deployment cycle. The granular pattern:

### A. Wake and First Hour

- **Wake time: 0515 ± 10 minutes**, without an alarm, every day. The body has not forgotten the household it was built in.
- She is out of the bunk inside ninety seconds. The bed is made — actually made, tight — within three minutes. This is not military training; it is her father's habit, transposed.
- **First movement: a perimeter walk** of whatever space she is in. Even in known-safe environments. She does not register it as a security action; it has been recoded as a wake-up routine. She will do this in a hotel, on a transport, in a kitchen. The route varies but the sequence is identical: doors, windows, exits, the location of her shield.
- **Coffee black, no sugar.** Drinks it standing. Luke's pattern, observed daily as an infant, locked in for life.
- **Reading.** She reads a printed news source — actual paper when available, screen when not — for fifteen minutes. She does not scroll; she reads top-to-bottom. Jessica's pattern.

### B. Physical Maintenance

The subject's enhanced physiology does not require conditioning to maintain baseline capacity, which makes her conditioning practice *psychologically* significant — it is not for the body, it is for the routine. Specifics:

- **Morning calisthenics, 0600–0645.** Bodyweight, not weights. Pushups in sets she stopped counting, pullups, a long static plank, then a shield-handling drill she has built herself — throwing the shield against a wall at angles that require it to return to a hand she has placed without looking. This is meditative more than functional.
- **Run, 0645–0730 when terrain permits.** She runs alone. She does not listen to music. This is the only block of the day reliably scheduled for her own interior, and a surveillance team would note that nothing else in the schedule competes for it.
- **No formal evening session.** The body recovers fast enough that maintenance is a one-block-a-day affair. The discipline is the point, not the volume.

### C. Eating Patterns

- **Three meals, usually, with high tolerance for missed ones.** Caloric demand is elevated due to enhanced physiology but she does not fuss about it; she eats what is in front of her, fast, and finishes.
- **Eats with her team whenever possible.** Will rearrange her schedule to be at the table. The team meal is non-negotiable in the way the family dinner was in the apartment she was raised in. This is the clearest direct inheritance from Luke.
- **Shares food off her plate.** Documented affiliation marker, traceable to age three. Adults notice it; she does not know she does it.
- **Hot sauce on most things.** Inherited preference, locked in early. A small bottle travels in her gear.
- **Does not drink alcohol in operational windows.** Will accept a beer in a clearly-off rotation, drinks it slowly, rarely a second.

### D. Workday Architecture

The adult workday, on a non-deployed cycle, decomposes into:

- **0800–1000: briefings and intelligence.** She is on time to the minute; she is, by team consensus, the one who arrives first and reads the packet before the meeting.
- **1000–1200: training, drills, or technical maintenance.** She is comfortable with hand-on-tool work; she sharpens, cleans, inspects gear. The shield is maintained personally — never handed off, never left out of arm's reach.
- **1200–1300: meal, team table.**
- **1300–1700: variable — coordination, planning, liaison, paperwork.** She is, surprisingly to those who first meet her, a competent administrator. She does not enjoy it but she does it correctly. This is her mother's pattern: the work that has to be done is done.
- **1700–1900: open block.** This is the block that reveals the subject. She rarely fills it with leisure. She reads, she walks, she trains again on her own, she calls no one. Solitude is her default downtime mode.
- **1900–2030: meal and informal team time.** She is present, warm, attentive, and does not lead the conversation. She laughs at others' jokes; she rarely tells one.
- **2030–2200: personal time.**
- **2200–2230: lights out.** Asleep inside ten minutes, almost always. The body does not litigate the day; it shuts down.

### E. Deployment Cycle

On deployment, the architecture collapses into the operational tempo, but certain elements survive:

- The perimeter walk happens on every new site within five minutes of arrival.
- The coffee, when available, is black, standing.
- The meal-with-the-team rule is held to within reason.
- Sleep is taken in short, dense blocks; she has the inherited ability to sleep on command in any posture, a skill she likely developed in the chaos-household of infancy where naps had to be taken when they could be taken.

### F. Communications and Personal Network

- **Phone use: minimal and instrumental.** She is not on social platforms in any persistent way. She uses messaging for operational coordination and short check-ins. She does not text for warmth; she shows up in person for warmth.
- **No reliably-recorded romantic partner.** Brief, undisclosed, undocumented relationships are likely; nothing publicly tracked. Her interpersonal heat is routed into the team-family unit.
- **Outreach to present-day Luke and Jessica:** the surveillance-relevant anomaly. She has met them. She does not initiate contact. When contact occurs, she manages her affect tightly, departs at the first non-awkward exit point, and does not refer to the meeting afterward. This is the single most consistent piece of avoidant behavior in her record.

### G. Rituals

The rituals — recurring behaviors with symbolic weight beyond their function — are the most revealing entries in the file:

- **The shield-clean.** Weekly, alone, taking approximately forty minutes. She inspects, cleans, and re-secures the straps. The act has the cadence of a religious observance. She does not speak during it. She does not let others observe it when she can help it.
- **A monthly day-off she rarely uses for rest.** When she takes it, she goes somewhere with water — a river, a harbor, a coast. She sits. She does not appear to be processing anything specific; she is permitting the silence the rest of the month does not allow.
- **The new-year mark.** On a date that does not correspond to any calendar holiday — most likely the anniversary of her parents' deaths in her own timeline, though she does not say — she eats alone and goes to bed early. Teammates have learned not to schedule her on this day.
- **Pre-mission stillness.** Two to three minutes, standing, eyes closed, before deployment. Not visible prayer, not visible meditation in any taught form; her own thing. Probably a list of names.
- **Post-mission food.** She insists on a meal with the team after every operation, regardless of hour or appetite. This is the most directly inherited ritual of all — the kitchen-counter handoff of her father, the dinner-with-the-phone-off rule, transposed into a war-room canteen forty years displaced.

### H. Tells and Tics (Adult)

The longitudinal continuity is striking. A surveillance file would underline these:

- The **room-scan on entry**, identical in sequence to the toddler version: left, right, up.
- **Touch-bias toward trusted persons remains paternal-coded** — she will put a hand on a shoulder, a hand on a back, the way her father did to her. She does not hug easily but the hand-on-shoulder is reliable.
- **Voice-seeking under stress** — when she needs grounding, she asks a question of a person whose voice she trusts. She does not need the answer; she needs the voice. Jessica's reading at bedtime, repurposed.
- **The food-sharing offer.** Still operational. Still unconscious. Still a reliable affiliation marker.
- **Color preference** trends toward warm earth tones in her off-duty clothing — yellows, mustards, browns. The shield is the only loud color she wears.
- **Right-hand dominance with a left-hand competence** — she can throw and catch the shield with either, but defaults right. Eats right-handed; writes right-handed; opens doors with the left to keep the right free. The last is trained.
- **A small, repeatable hand-flex** before contact: she opens and closes her right hand twice. The body's pre-flight check. Her father's tell, almost certainly observed in infancy and locked in.
- **She does not flinch.** Not at loud sounds, not at sudden movements, not at threats. This is partly physiology and partly conditioning. It reads to strangers as either reassuring or unsettling depending on what they brought into the room with them.

---

## IV. A Week of Surveillance, Composited

A representative week, if a team were assigned to her on a low-tempo cycle:

- **Monday.** Wake 0512. Perimeter walk. Coffee, paper. Calisthenics, run (river path, 5.4 miles, no variation in pace). Shower. Briefing block 0800. Drills 1000. Lunch with three teammates at the canteen table; she sits with her back to a wall, not deliberately, but consistently. Afternoon paperwork. 1700 block: she walks, alone, for an hour, in a direction with no apparent destination. Dinner with team. 2030 block: reads (a printed book, military history or biography, not fiction). Lights out 2207.
- **Tuesday.** Identical wake. Identical morning. A non-routine event mid-morning — an unexpected visitor, a system alert — she handles it with no visible elevation in arousal, then returns to the prior task within a minute. The continuity is the surveillance-relevant fact.
- **Wednesday.** Shield-clean evening. Door closed. No teammate enters. Out at 2150. Lights out 2210.
- **Thursday.** Deployment short-notice. The architecture collapses cleanly; the team is moving within thirty minutes. She is the second one to the transport. She sleeps on the flight, upright, for forty minutes, wakes without grogginess.
- **Friday.** Returned. Post-mission meal. Eats more than usual. Speaks less than usual. Lights out early.
- **Saturday.** Off-block. She is somewhere with water. She is not reachable for six hours. She returns by evening without explanation and no one asks.
- **Sunday.** Family-dinner energy at the team table — a longer meal, looser, she stays at the table past her usual exit point. This is the closest the week comes to the Harlem apartment she was raised in. She does not know that's what it is.

---

## V. Synthesis

The surveillance record makes one thing irrefutable: **the household she was raised in is still running, daily, in the body of the adult operator she became.** The hand-flex, the room-scan, the meal-with-the-team, the bedside-voice need, the shared food, the morning coffee taken standing, the bathtub protocol generalized into a perimeter walk — these are not coincidences. They are the load-bearing residue of two competent, loving, embattled parents who got a finite number of years to build something into their daughter before history took them. The structure they built is the structure she runs on. A week of surveillance would log, beneath the operator, **the child who was taught well, and who remembered.**


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

The subject's family-of-origin file is unusually short in personnel count and unusually long in case-density per person. There are, effectively, four primary nodes: Luke Cage (father), Jessica Jones (mother), and — in the displaced future — the *absence* of both that constitutes its own active relationship. Then a perimeter ring of fictive kin who functioned as family in the operational sense (Jarvis, Squirrel Girl, Danny Rand). Siblings: none on record. Children: none on record. The dead, in her case, are her parents — but only in one timeline. In the present timeline she is the dead of no one and the daughter of the living. This bifurcation is the central fact of the file.

---

## 1. Luke Cage (Carl Lucas) — Father

**History of the bond.** From the natal record forward, Luke is the parent who *holds*. The visual canon across Bendis's *New Avengers* and Walker's *Power Man and Iron Fist* is consistent and specific: Luke is the one with the child on his shoulder, on his hip, in the bend of his elbow, asleep against his chest mid-conversation. He changes her, feeds her, narrates the room to her, takes her on errands in Harlem in a stroller while conducting business that would, in any other operator's life, require her absence. He does not absent her; he integrates her. The behavioral signature is rare and worth naming: a Black father, formerly incarcerated, whose entire adult arc is the construction of a domesticity the carceral system tried to deny him — and the daughter is the proof-of-work.

**Dynamics.** Luke is the regulatory baseline of the household. Jessica spikes; Luke holds level. The child grows up watching her mother's affect-weather pass over her father's stable barometric pressure, and learns from this that *calm is a choice one makes for the people one loves*. Adult-Dani's affect regulation — composed, low-baseline, warmable on demand — is Luke's affect regulation, transcribed. The voice in her head when she needs to compose herself is, almost certainly, his cadence.

**Trust / safety.** Absolute, never tested, never broken in the child record. The single most safety-coded body in her early environment. Even his subdermal hardness is, behaviorally, a *cradle* in her infant experience — the unbreakable surface against which she sleeps. The Burstein process, in another household, produced a weapon; in hers, produced a crib.

**Evolution into adulthood (future timeline).** Adult-Dani speaks of him in past tense with the particular quiet of a daughter who has buried a father she liked. Her chosen weapon — the shield — is conspicuously *not* his identity (unbreakable skin, the body as armor). She refuses to be Power Woman. The refusal is not rejection; it is *protection of the memory*. To take up his powers as identity would be to overwrite the man with the function. She keeps the man intact by carrying someone else's symbol.

**Failure mode.** The pressure point Luke creates is the high baseline he set for what a father is. Any future partner she might consider for co-parenting is being measured, unconsciously, against a man who carried her through gunfire with one arm and a bottle in the other. The standard is unfair and she will not name it. Secondary failure mode: in the present timeline, encountering living Luke, she is at risk of the *quiet collapse* — not a scene, but a moment alone after which she will need forty-eight hours to reassemble. The canon does not show this. The canon's silence on it is itself the datum.

**Leverage.** Anyone who knows the bond knows the lever. Threats to Luke in the present timeline would override her mission discipline faster than threats to herself. She has presumably war-gamed this and constructed protocols; the protocols would not survive contact with the actual event.

---

## 2. Jessica Jones (Jones-Cage) — Mother

**History of the bond.** Jessica is the parent who *sees*. Where Luke holds, Jessica scans — the perimeter, the room, the child's face, the threat-vector through the window. The mother-child dyad is built on a different substrate than the father-child one: Luke gives the child *containment*, Jessica gives the child *intelligence about the world*. Bendis's *Jessica Jones* (2016) opens with Jessica running a faked separation to infiltrate a threat, using Dani's removal from the home as cover. This is not absence; it is *Jessica's particular form of presence* — the mother who will burn down the house's appearances to protect what the house contains. The child, once old enough to understand, will read this correctly: *my mother lied to the world to keep me; my mother lies very well; my mother's lies are a perimeter*.

**Dynamics.** Jessica is the parent Dani most resembles cognitively. The PI's pattern-recognition, the refusal to trust narrative until evidence has been collected, the comfort with operating under cover, the willingness to use unloved methods for loved ends — all transcribed into adult-Dani's operational style. The Captain America of 20XX runs missions like a private investigator: she gathers, she watches, she moves once, decisively. This is not Steve Rogers' methodology. It is her mother's.

**Trust / safety.** Trust: total. Safety: differently calibrated than with Luke. Jessica is the parent whose *presence* the child experienced as protective and whose *fear* the child experienced as informational. Children of trauma survivors learn to read the parent's nervous system as a weather station. Dani's hypervigilance — present at low, integrated levels in the adult, never disabling — is Jessica's hypervigilance, inherited socially as much as genetically.

**The pregnancy datum.** Jessica was a powered, combat-active operator throughout the pregnancy under federal scrutiny conditions (*Civil War* window). The womb itself was a high-cortisol environment. Whether this contributes to adult-Dani's exceptional stress tolerance (*in utero* habituation) or to a latent dysregulation not yet surfaced is undetermined by canon. A surveillance file would flag it; a clinician would watch for it.

**Evolution into adulthood (future timeline).** Jessica is the harder loss. The mother-daughter axis in this family is the one through which the child learned *how the world actually works* — and losing the person who teaches you the world is a different epistemological event than losing the person who held you. Adult-Dani's composed past-tense on Jessica is, I suspect, more effortful than her past-tense on Luke. The mother is the harder ghost because the mother is the *operating manual*.

**Failure mode.** Dani has inherited Jessica's pattern of *running cover stories on people she loves to protect them*. This was protective in childhood (it kept her safe). It is a liability in adult intimacy (it prevents being known). The probable adult expression: Dani will under-disclose to teammates and lovers under the rationalization of operational security, when the actual function is the avoidance of being seen at the seams.

**Leverage.** Present-timeline Jessica is, for adult-Dani, the *forbidden room*. To allow herself the mother she has buried is to undo the grief solution that lets her function. Anyone wanting to break Dani would not threaten Jessica — they would arrange for Dani and Jessica to be alone together in a quiet room for an hour. The composure would not survive it. This is the single most exploitable interior in the file.

---

## 3. The Absence of Both — The Dead as Active Relationship

**The relationship that is a relationship.** In the displaced future, both parents are gone. The mode of their deaths is not crisply detailed in canon, which is its own evidence — Dani does not narrate the manner. She narrates the *fact*. A clinician reads narrative refusal as boundary-setting around an unhealed surface.

**Function in the personality.** The dead parents are the *load* the role carries. Captain America's shield is not, for her, a national symbol primarily; it is a container for two specific ghosts. The principle is the vessel by which the people are kept. This is the introjective mourning solution named in the workup, and it is functioning — but functioning is not the same as resolved.

**Behavioral signature.** Watch for these tells in the longitudinal record: (a) refusal of solo downtime longer than 36 hours, (b) tendency to schedule debriefs or training to fill calendrical gaps, (c) discomfort with anniversaries (birthdays, calendar dates corresponding to known family events), (d) selective deafness to questions phrased "tell me about your mother / father" versus willingness to answer "what did your mother / father do in situation X" — the *operational* mode of recall is open, the *affective* mode is closed.

**Failure mode.** The grief solution requires the world to keep needing her. Peace is the threat. A long stretch without mission is the condition under which the dead would arrive in the room.

**Leverage.** Already named: the present-timeline parents. Also: any operator who fought alongside Luke or Jessica in the future timeline, if extant, would constitute both the most-trusted possible ally and the most-destabilizing possible presence. She would not refuse such a person; she would also not be intact around them.

---

## 4. Edwin Jarvis — Fictive Kin (Grandfather Role)

**History.** Jarvis is the household's institutional grandparent across the Bendis *New Avengers* era — present at the Tower, present at bottle-warmings, present at the handoffs that allowed both parents to leave the room. The child experiences him as a *reliable adult body of unconditional benevolence*, the slot a grandparent normally fills.

**The Skrull rupture.** During *Secret Invasion*, the Jarvis who took the infant was an impostor. The child has no narrative memory of this; the *household* has structural memory of it. Jessica's hypervigilance around handoffs after this event almost certainly tightens. The child, too young to remember the event, inherits the *aftermath* — a household in which "people you trust may not be who they appear" is a working postulate, transmitted through her mother's micro-behaviors at every doorway.

**Evolution.** Post-recovery, the real Jarvis re-integrates into the caregiving network without canonical evidence of permanent disruption — a tribute to the family's restorative capacity. But the file notes a permanent *background* lesson the child absorbs: *the grandfather can be replaced by a hostile facsimile and you will not know until after the fact*. This is the seed of a particular adult posture toward authority figures — warm, available, perimeter-checked.

**Trust / leverage.** Trust: high, with a low-grade verification subroutine that never fully shuts off. Leverage: minor. Jarvis is not a pressure point because the child's relationship to him was already structured by his having once not-been-him.

---

## 5. Squirrel Girl (Doreen Green) — Fictive Kin (Older Sister / Nanny)

**History.** Doreen is the actual nanny across a meaningful stretch of toddlerhood (*New Avengers* vol. 2). She brings into the household something neither parent has in abundance and both recognize as necessary: *unguarded joy*. The household is loving but not light. Doreen is light.

**Function.** She is the developmental counterweight to the ambient threat-environment. The child's capacity to *laugh easily* — present in adult-Dani, who connects warmly on demand — is plausibly seeded here. Doreen also models *competence-without-grimness*, which the parental units cannot fully provide because their competence was forged in grimness.

**Trust / evolution.** Total in childhood. In the future timeline, Doreen's status is unspecified. If alive in Dani's future, she would be among the very small number of people Dani could be *unguarded* around. If dead, she is part of the load.

**Failure mode / leverage.** Minor. The relationship is a *protective factor* in the file, not a pressure point.

---

## 6. Danny Rand (Iron Fist) — Fictive Kin (Uncle)

**History.** Danny is Luke's brother-by-choice across decades of canon, and that brotherhood is transmitted to the child as uncle-hood. He is in the household, around the table, in the rotation of trustworthy adult bodies. He brings into Dani's developmental field something specific: *disciplined mysticism*, the model of a man whose competence is the product of years of structured practice rather than chemical accident or trauma.

**Function.** Danny is, plausibly, the model from which Dani draws her later willingness to inhabit a *symbolic role* — Iron Fist is the bearer of a tradition, not just a fighter. To take up Captain America's shield is to do, for a nation, what Danny does for K'un-Lun: be the current vessel of a continuous principle. The introjective grief solution may have a Danny-shaped template under it.

**Trust / evolution / leverage.** Trust: high. Future-timeline status unspecified. Leverage: as with Doreen, more protective factor than pressure point.

---

## 7. Siblings — Negative Finding

No siblings on record. The only-child status compounds every pattern above: the inheritor function is undiluted, the family burden is undivided, the grief in the future timeline is unshared. A sibling would have been the one person who could metabolize the parents' deaths *with* her on equal terms. The absence of that person is a structural feature of her aloneness as Captain America. The team-as-family adaptation is, in part, the manufacture of the siblings she did not have.

---

## 8. Children — Negative Finding

No children on record in either timeline. The negative finding is operationally significant: a person whose entire developmental file is organized around *being the protected child of protective parents who became the protective inheritor* has not yet been observed in the role her parents occupied toward her. Whether she would replicate the integration Luke and Jessica achieved — child-in-the-stroller-during-the-firefight — or whether she would over-correct into either total exclusion or total exposure is unknown and, given her likely future, may remain unknown. The role of parent is the untested chamber of her personality. It is also the only role in which the grief solution would be forced to reorganize: a child of her own would demand presence in a register the shield cannot occupy.

---

## 9. Family-System Synthesis

The Cage-Jones household is, in family-systems terms, a *high-cohesion, high-adaptability* unit operating under chronic external threat. Both parents are trauma survivors who built, against considerable odds, a functional and loving co-parenting dyad. The child was raised inside a perimeter that was tight, warm, and broad — staffed by superhuman fictive kin, organized around her safety as a stated household priority, and never closed off from the world her parents fought in. She was neither hidden from the work nor exposed to it carelessly; she was *integrated* into a household whose work was the world.

The adult product of this household, in the worst-case future, lost the household and kept the integration. She became a one-person continuation of the perimeter her parents maintained — a household of one, holding the line her parents held, with a symbol drawn from outside the family because the symbols *inside* the family were too painful to wear. The shield is the family business conducted under a borrowed name.

The leverage map, summarized:
- **Highest-yield pressure point:** unstructured time alone with present-timeline Jessica.
- **Second:** unstructured time alone with present-timeline Luke.
- **Third:** any forced inactivity exceeding the duration her defenses are calibrated for.
- **Fourth:** the death of a teammate under her command — the first real stress-test of the introjective grief solution.
- **Background:** any reactivation of the Skrull-substitution schema (people-are-not-who-they-appear), which would route through her mother's nervous system as inherited.

The protective factors, summarized:
- A father whose calm she carries as her own.
- A mother whose intelligence she carries as her method.
- A fictive-kin network that taught her the team-as-family pattern she now relies on.
- The absence, in the record, of any primary attachment failure. The foundation, as previously assessed, held.

What the family gave her is what she is using to carry what the family's loss cost her. The accounting balances — for now, and at a cost that has not yet been paid in full.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

The subject's relational topology is unusual in its breadth and its rotation. Most operators of her cohort have a primary dyad and a small handful of trusted seconds; Danielle Cage carries, across both timeline-phases of her record, a distributed network of fifteen-plus deeply-trusted figures, with no single primary attachment outside her parents. The pattern is legible: she was raised inside a *roster*. The Avengers Tower nursery was a hot-desk for super-powered caregivers; her infant face was, at one point or another, held by every major hero of the Bendis-era roster. The internal working model that takes shape from such a childhood is *team-as-kin*, and her adult relational style is its mature expression. What follows is the sub-by-sub mapping.

---

## DOREEN GREEN — "Aunt Squirrel"

**History.** Squirrel Girl is the foundational non-parental caregiver. Engaged in *New Avengers* vol. 2 as Dani's primary daytime nanny during the post-*Siege* reconstitution of the Avengers — Luke and Jessica both on active rotation, Doreen presented as the most over-qualified babysitter in the city (single-handed defeats of Doom, Thanos, MODOK on her résumé). The arrangement runs for roughly eighteen months of in-story time. She is, statistically, the human face Dani saw most often in her second year of life after her parents.

**Dynamics.** Doreen's caregiving signature is *unembarrassed delight*. She narrates, sings, plays high-energy games, treats Dani as a complete person rather than a project. Crucially, she also exposes Dani to the squirrel network — there are background panels of Dani being chittered at by Tippy-Toe and reaching for squirrels with no startle response. The infant's first lesson in *strange creatures are not necessarily threats* is taught by Doreen.

**Evolution.** Doreen ages out of the daily-nanny role as the Cage-Jones family relocates to Hell's Kitchen and then briefly out of state. The relationship persists as warm-extended-family — birthday appearances, drop-bys, the kind of aunt who shows up with a worryingly large plush toy. In the adult timeline, the canon does not specify Doreen's fate, but adult-Dani's affect when present-day Doreen is in the room (inferable from her adjacent appearances) is the softest she gets with anyone outside the parental dyad.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1. Of every figure outside her parents, Doreen is the one Dani would, in extremis, hand a child to without hesitation — a transitive property: the person who held *her* safely is the person who holds *the held* safely.

**Failure mode.** Doreen's tactical optimism — *we can talk this out, even with Galactus* — does not always survive contact with the kind of opponents adult-Dani fights. If they were ever on a mission together where Doreen's preferred resolution failed catastrophically, Dani would have to choose between the inheritance of Squirrel Girl's worldview (every monster is a misunderstood person) and the inheritance of her mother's (some people are just the threat they appear to be). She would side with Jessica. Doreen would not hold the disagreement against her; Dani would hold it against herself.

**Leverage.** Anyone who could credibly threaten Doreen would have, for two or three seconds, the entirety of Dani's tactical attention. That is a long window.

---

## DANNY RAND — "Uncle Danny"

**History.** Iron Fist is Luke's brother-by-choice; that makes Danny, by household convention, Dani's uncle in the operative sense. He is in and out of the apartment throughout her childhood — *Power Man and Iron Fist* (Walker, 2016) is largely set in a Harlem where Danny is a fixture. She is named, in part, in a soft echo of him: *Danielle* / *Danny*. The naming is acknowledged in dialogue.

**Dynamics.** Danny's mode with Dani is *teacher-uncle*. He shows her chi-breathing exercises framed as games, recites K'un-Lun fables as bedtime stories, gives her a small ceremonial something — a token, a stone — that becomes a comfort object. Luke teases him for being insufferable about it; Jessica permits it because Danny is, of all the family's circle, the one most reliably *gentle*. He has never raised a voice in the apartment.

**Evolution.** The relationship deepens through her childhood from babysitter-uncle to *first martial instructor*. The earliest forms — stance, breath, the discipline of stillness — almost certainly originate with Danny rather than with either parent. This shapes the adult operator's signature: her combat carriage carries a center-of-gravity discipline that is neither Luke's brawler-rooting nor Jessica's brawler-improvisation. There is a *trained stillness* under it. That is Danny's fingerprint.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1. The unique value Danny adds is *philosophical*. He is the household member who answers the *why* questions without flinching and without lying. When child-Dani asks, around age four, why her parents go into danger, the canon-adjacent inference is that it was Danny who gave her the answer she actually accepted.

**Failure mode.** Danny's periodic disappearances into K'un-Lun business are absences that the adult Dani would not name as wounds but that would have shaped her tolerance for *people leaving and coming back changed*. He is also her template for *the powerful man who underclaims* — which, mirror-imaged, is partly her own pattern. If Danny ever ideologically broke with her in adulthood, she would be more shaken than she would be by an equivalent break from any other peer.

**Leverage.** A K'un-Lun crisis that pulled Danny in over his head would draw her in unhesitatingly, regardless of mission load.

---

## ROBERTO DA COSTA — Field Commander, Adopted Brother

**History.** Sunspot is the figure who *receives* the time-displaced adult Dani in the present timeline (*U.S.Avengers*, Ewing, 2017). She arrives as a stranger from a possible future; he integrates her into the team without bureaucratic friction and treats her, from week one, as load-bearing. This is the single most important non-natal relational event in her adult record.

**Dynamics.** Roberto's signature is *expansive welcome wrapped around ruthless operational clarity*. He flatters, jokes, lavishes resources, and underneath it all is a man who has assessed her capabilities inside an hour and is deploying them at full extension. Dani recognizes the pattern immediately — it is her mother's *blunt-affect-with-soft-underbelly* in a Brazilian-billionaire register. She trusts it inside the first deployment.

**Evolution.** The relationship moves from commander-subordinate to *co-leads* and then to *chosen siblings* over the run. Roberto is the first person in her adult life who insists she rest, who notices when she has not eaten, who reads the *flatness* of her affect as the alarm signal it is and does not let her get away with it. He is, functionally, the older brother she did not get to have.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1, and uniquely so — Roberto is one of the only people who has been allowed access to the *present-tense feelings about her dead future-parents* zone. Whether he is told the full content or just permitted to sit beside her while she is in it is unclear; the access is what matters.

**Failure mode.** Roberto's appetite for *winning at the institutional game* — running A.I.M., maneuvering against Hydra, playing the longer board — occasionally cuts against the directness she prefers. She follows him through it; she does not love it. If he ever ordered her to do something that violated the Steve-Rogers-shield-theology she has chosen to carry, she would refuse, the team would fracture, and she would be the one bleeding internally about it for years.

**Leverage.** Threats to Roberto are threats to her. Threats by Roberto to the *frame* of the team-as-family are the more dangerous category.

---

## SAMUEL GUTHRIE — Cannonball, Steady-Second

**History.** Sam is the other senior figure on Roberto's roster and Dani's most regular field partner during the run. Their working relationship coheres fast — within three or four ops they are operating in the no-talk-required register that takes most pairs a year.

**Dynamics.** Sam is the Kentucky-farm-boy steadiness that Dani's nervous system reads as *safe* in the way her father's calm reads as safe. He is unhurried, profoundly competent, and entirely unimpressed by the shield-as-symbol — he treats her as a person who happens to carry it. This is precisely the registration she needs from a peer and almost no one offers.

**Evolution.** The bond stabilizes into a *quiet trust* — the kind of partnership where neither party feels they need to check in to know the other is still there. Sam is, among her cohort, the figure most likely to become her *eventual sounding board for the unprocessed grief*. He has the patience for it. Whether she has yet allowed it is uncertain.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1 operational, Tier 2 emotional — only because she has not yet opened the latter door, not because Sam wouldn't hold it.

**Failure mode.** Sam's family-first instincts could conflict with her mission-first instincts in a scenario requiring her to send him into a likely-fatal position. She would do it. He would go. The cost would land later.

**Leverage.** Harm to Sam's siblings (Husk, Icarus, etc.) would draw her in as an auxiliary; harm to Sam directly would compromise her tactical judgment for the duration.

---

## VICTOR ALVAREZ — Power Man, the Younger-Brother Inheritance

**History.** Victor is a complicated figure in her childhood record — Luke's named successor in the Power Man identity, a teenager during her toddlerhood, in and out of the Cage household during the *Power Man and Iron Fist* run. He is not a caregiver; he is a *young man her father is mentoring*, which makes him an older-brother figure she watches at a distance.

**Dynamics.** Child-Dani's relationship with Victor is observational and affectionate; he is gentle with her, but his primary axis in the household is *protégé to Luke*. She absorbs, watching them, the template of *how her father teaches a young person who is not her*. This is data: it tells her, at a developmental moment when she is forming theories about her father's interior, that *Luke chooses to invest in young people who need it*. She files this. As an adult, in the displaced timeline, it likely informs how she handles the protégés who attach to her.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 2. Affectionate but not load-bearing.

**Failure mode.** None obvious — the relationship was bounded by her age and his role.

**Leverage.** Low-direct, moderate-indirect: he is part of the *Harlem network* whose collective wellbeing would draw her response.

---

## EDWIN JARVIS — The Household's Institutional Memory

**History.** Jarvis is, depending on the era, intermittent or constant — but he is the figure whose *presence in the building* defined the Avengers Tower nursery years. He held her as an infant. He also, in the form of a Skrull impostor, *took her* as an infant and was the locus of one of the household's foundational ruptures (*Secret Invasion*).

**Dynamics.** The relationship is layered: Jarvis is unimpeachably loving in his actual person, and unimpeachably *the face of betrayal* in his impostor's. The Cage household's reckoning with this was, by all evidence, generous — the real Jarvis was re-welcomed without reservation. But the household-as-system internalized a lesson: *the most trusted face can be a mask*. Dani was too young to encode the event explicitly, but she was old enough to encode the post-event *vigilance recalibration* her mother carried into every subsequent caregiving handoff.

**Evolution.** Jarvis becomes, across her childhood, the *institutional grandfather* — a figure of unfailing gentleness, tea, mild humor, and quiet competence. He is the model of *how to be powerful by being reliable*. That model has fingerprints on the adult operator's relational signature.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1, with a thin asterisk that she may not consciously carry but that her nervous system might. She is more likely than the average operator to verify identity in soft ways — phrasing checks, gesture checks — when greeting trusted figures she has not seen in some time. This is Jessica's habit, inherited.

**Failure mode.** Jarvis's mortality. He is older than every other figure in this section. The day he is gone, she will register it as the loss of a continent.

**Leverage.** Low — his vulnerability is age, not adversarial.

---

## CAROL DANVERS — The Standard Set, The Distance Kept

**History.** Carol is in the apartment, on the roster, in the rotation throughout Dani's infancy and childhood. She is also, of all the heroes Dani grows up around, the one closest to *what Dani will become*: a powered woman carrying a symbol-mantle. The resonance is structural before it is conscious.

**Dynamics.** Carol's mode with child-Dani is *cool affection* — she is not a natural caregiver in the Squirrel Girl register, but she is reliable, capable, and present. She is also the figure most likely to be holding Dani when something explodes on the news in the background, which means she is the figure most associated with *the adult who keeps holding you while the world catches fire*. That is its own kind of attachment data.

**Evolution.** In the adult displaced timeline, Carol is — depending on the future-state — either dead, missing, or the figure Dani most consciously models on. The choice of the Captain America shield over Captain Marvel's mantle is itself a datum: she had the option of stepping into Carol's frame and chose Steve's. The most defensible read is that Steve's mantle is *less personal* — taking Carol's would have been taking the place of a woman she loved as a child, taking Steve's is taking up a flag.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1 in childhood, complicated in adulthood. The present-day Carol is one of the figures the time-displaced adult Dani most carefully manages her affect around. There is unfinished business in that gaze.

**Failure mode.** A direct conflict — *Civil War II*-style — between Carol's institutional certainty and Dani's people-first ethic would force her to take a side, and she would take her mother's old side, against Carol. She would not enjoy it.

**Leverage.** Moderate — emotionally significant, but Carol is the figure most able to handle being weaponized against, which limits the actual exposure.

---

## JESSICA DREW — The Other Jessica

**History.** Spider-Woman is one of Jessica Jones's closest peers across the Bendis era, and a constant in the Cage-Jones household's social map. She is *the other Jessica* — the namesake confusion was a running joke in the apartment. She holds Dani frequently in infancy, is on the short list of trusted emergency-handlers, and is, importantly, the *friend of her mother* whose presence taught Dani that her mother *has friends* — that adults form chosen-kin too.

**Dynamics.** Drew's mode is *wry-protective*. She does not perform parental warmth; she performs *aunt-who-will-handle-it*. The relationship's signature is a low-grade ongoing affection that does not require maintenance.

**Evolution.** Stable across the childhood record. In the adult timeline, unspecified.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 2 — high trust, low intimacy. She is the model for how Dani handles her own *Jessica Drew–equivalents* as an adult: peer-aunts whom she loves without performing it.

**Failure mode.** Drew has her own history of impostor-substitution (the Skrull queen). The Cage household carries two separate *trusted-woman-was-secretly-a-Skrull* events. Dani's vigilance baseline is non-coincidental.

**Leverage.** Low-direct, moderate via the Jessica-network.

---

## MISTY KNIGHT & COLLEEN WING — The Harlem Aunts

**History.** Misty is Harlem's other institution; Colleen is her partner-in-Heroes-for-Hire. Both are in the orbit throughout Dani's childhood, especially during the *Power Man and Iron Fist* run, where they are professionally entangled with Luke and Danny.

**Dynamics.** Misty's mode is *no-nonsense aunt with a bionic arm and zero patience for excuses*. Colleen's is *the calm one who will actually answer a child's question*. Together they form a secondary aunt-pair complementary to Doreen — where Doreen is delight, Misty/Colleen are *competence under pressure made visible*.

**Evolution.** The relationships stabilize as part of Dani's Harlem-network. They are the figures she would, in adulthood, drop in on without an appointment.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1 (Misty), Tier 2 (Colleen — less daily contact).

**Failure mode.** Misty's police-adjacent past and institutional pragmatism could, in a high-stakes moment, push for a procedural resolution Dani's ethics would reject. The break would be heated and reparable.

**Leverage.** High via Harlem-as-perimeter; harm to Misty would be processed as harm to family.

---

## JAMES "LOGAN" HOWLETT — The Quiet Periphery

**History.** Wolverine is roster-adjacent throughout Dani's infancy on the New Avengers and is documented holding her on at least one occasion. He is not a daily caregiver but is, notably, one of the figures Luke specifically *trusts* with her — a non-trivial fact given Luke's discriminating standards.

**Dynamics.** Logan's mode with infants is famously gentle and famously brief; he holds, he hands back, he leaves the room. With Dani specifically, the documented interaction is *grandfatherly* in register — the centuries-old man who recognizes a new beginning and treats it with appropriate reverence.

**Evolution.** Sparse but persistent. Logan is the figure Dani would, in any timeline, recognize across a crowded room without having seen him in years.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 2, with disproportionate emotional weight.

**Failure mode.** Logan's tendency to *take the burden himself* — to absorb the cost of a decision so that no one else has to — is a model Dani has inherited. If he ever asked her to let him do it one last time, she would not be able to refuse.

**Leverage.** Surprisingly high for the contact frequency.

---

## MATT MURDOCK — The Other Hell's Kitchen Door

**History.** During the period the Cage-Jones family lives in Hell's Kitchen, Matt is a known quantity — a neighborhood institution, a sometime co-counsel for Jessica's legal matters, an occasional presence at the building. He is not avuncular; he is *adjacent in the geography*.

**Dynamics.** With child-Dani, Matt is courteous, slightly formal, the kind of adult who treats children as people requiring honest answers. The relationship is bounded but warm.

**Evolution.** Persistent low-grade contact.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 2.

**Failure mode.** Matt's Catholic-guilt operational style and willingness to martyr is foreign to her temperament; she would resist any attempt by him to make her complicit in his self-destruction.

**Leverage.** Moderate via the Hell's Kitchen network.

---

## PETER PARKER — The Older Cousin

**History.** Spider-Man is in and out of the Avengers Tower years as one of Luke's closest team-friends. He holds Dani, jokes around her, is one of the few heroes whose presence she finds *funny* from a young age. The relationship is light and warm.

**Dynamics.** Peter is the model of *the powerful person who deflects with humor* — a coping style Dani does not inherit (she inherits Jessica's deflect-with-dryness instead), but recognizes and is comforted by. He is the older-cousin figure.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 2, with no ceiling — would escalate instantly under stress.

**Failure mode.** Peter's chronic over-extension is a pattern she would worry about as an adult; if she had to choose between letting him exhaust himself and forcibly benching him, she would attempt the latter and probably fail.

**Leverage.** Moderate.

---

## TONI HO, ROBERTO REYES, AIKKU JOKINEN, DOREEN GREEN (U.S.AVENGERS COHORT) — The Younger Roster

**History.** Adult-Dani's working family in the present timeline is the U.S.Avengers roster: Toni Ho (Iron Patriot), Roberto Reyes (Ghost Rider), Aikku (Enigma/Pod), and the returning Doreen. They are her *peers-and-juniors* — the team she is technically senior to in symbol-weight but co-equal to in operational reality.

**Dynamics.** Her mode with them is *older-sister-who-was-once-the-youngest* — she remembers being the kid in the room with the big roster, and she actively works to make sure the juniors feel like principals. Toni Ho is the figure she most consciously mentors; Reyes is the one she most consciously *contains* (he runs hot, she is the cooler hand on the wheel); Aikku she treats with a particular gentleness that suggests she reads the alien-symbiote situation as analogous to her own *not-quite-baseline-human* embodiment.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1 collective, with Toni Ho as the individual closest.

**Failure mode.** Losing one of them in the field is the *single most likely event* to crack her composed-grief carapace. It has not yet been tested on-page.

**Leverage.** Maximum. They are the perimeter.

---

## STEVE ROGERS — The Mantle's Ghost

**History.** Adult-Dani never knew Steve in any reliable register — in her future timeline he is dead before she takes the shield. The present-day Steve, when she encounters him, is a stranger she has spent her adulthood becoming.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is *posthumous even when he is in the room*. She defers to him, learns from him, and is unable to entirely meet his eye in the register he offers — because for her he is a *frame*, not a person. The interaction is unfailingly correct and quietly painful.

**Trust / Safety.** Tier 1 ideologically, Tier 3 personally — they have not built the latter and the structure makes building it awkward.

**Failure mode.** A direct disagreement with Steve over the shield's use would destabilize her self-concept more than a physical defeat could. She has organized her identity around *being the inheritor of his line*; being told by him that she is misusing it would be cataclysmic.

**Leverage.** Symbolic — anyone who could turn Steve against her, or stage the appearance of it, would have a uniquely effective weapon.

---

## SYNTHETIC READ ON THE NETWORK

The relational architecture is *redundant by design*. No single figure outside her parents is load-bearing in the way primary attachments usually are; instead, the load is distributed across roughly a dozen Tier-1 and Tier-2 figures, with the *team* as the actual primary attachment unit. This is the mature expression of the rotating-caregiver childhood: she does not need any one person to be everything, because no one ever was. She needs the *room* to be full


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

A note on scope before the rolls: the subject's life is bifurcated, and the population of this section is correspondingly thin and asymmetric. Child-Dani has no rivals, no enemies in any operational sense, and obviously no lovers — what she has are *people who came for her*, a closed list. Adult-Dani, the Captain America of 20XX, has the inverse problem: a future-history of antagonists most of whom are off-page, inferable from her affect when she names them and from the silhouettes that Ewing and Remender sketched but never colored in. This section therefore mixes the *documented* (those who appear on panel against her or in her timeline) with the *substantiated-by-pattern* — the kind of inference a long-running surveillance file makes when the subject reacts to a name with a specific micro-tightening of the jaw.

Lovers are the thinnest category in canon. The file is honest about that and reads the absence as a datum.

---

## RIVALS

### Roberto Da Costa (Sunspot) — rival-in-command, the only person who outranks her on her own team

Da Costa is not an enemy and not, in any conventional sense, a rival — he is her commanding officer at A.I.M.-reformed / U.S.Avengers, the man who recruited her out of time-displacement and gave her a uniform inside the present tense. But the relationship contains a rivalrous load that is the closest thing in her adult life to an unresolved status conflict, and the file logs it as such.

*History.* Da Costa pulls her into the team in *U.S.Avengers* (2017) knowing exactly what she is — a future Captain America, displaced, orphaned, carrying a shield that in the present is held by other hands. He treats her with a particular blend of deference and managerial pragmatism: he *defers to the symbol* in public, *manages the operator* in private. She accepts the structure without visible friction.

*Dynamic.* The rivalry, such as it is, is jurisdictional and unspoken. She is, by experience-density, the most combat-tested American on the roster — she has fought wars he has only briefed on. He is, by institutional position, her boss. He is also a billionaire-class strategist with a flair she does not share; he runs operations with theatrical confidence; she executes with compressed efficiency. The team reads them as complementary; the file reads a low-grade ongoing negotiation about whose tempo wins when they disagree. She almost always defers. He almost always lets her shape the tactical call once committed. The arrangement works because both are mature; it would not work if either were not.

*Trust / safety.* High and increasing. He has not put her in a position she could not extract from. She has not refused an order. Trust is *operational* — she would not bring him a personal disclosure, and he has the good sense not to ask for one.

*Failure mode.* If Da Costa's strategic frame ever required *spending* her — accepting her death or capture as the cost of an objective — the relationship would not survive his decision and she would not survive his decision. She would obey; she would not forgive in time to matter.

*Leverage.* He holds her present-tense legitimacy. Without his platform she is a time-displaced operator with no jurisdiction. She holds, in return, the symbolic weight that lets his unit make claims it could not otherwise make. The leverage is mutual, balanced, and quietly understood by both.

### Steve Rogers — the predecessor who is also present-tense alive

Not a rival in any combative sense and emphatically not an enemy — and yet the most psychologically loaded peer-relationship in her file, because he is *the man whose shield she carries and who is, in her present, still carrying it himself*. The arrangement is unprecedented and produces a specific affective texture every time they share a room.

*History.* In her timeline he is dead or retired and the shield came to her under conditions she has not narrated. In the present timeline he is operational, vigorous, and — crucially — *kind to her*. He has not asked for the shield back. He has not condescended. He has, in the few logged interactions, treated her as a peer-in-the-role with a separate jurisdiction.

*Dynamic.* She watches him the way an apprentice watches a master she has already become without his help. There is reverence; there is also a quiet refusal to be schooled. She does not seek his counsel. She does not avoid him either. The micro-pattern: when he enters a tactical space she is operating in, her shoulders square *down* rather than up — the body-language of someone who refuses to perform deference and refuses to perform challenge.

*Trust / safety.* Total in the operational frame. She would take a bullet for him and expects he would for her. The intimate frame is unused — she has not let him be a mentor to her, which is the single most diagnostic choice in her adult interpersonal record. To accept his mentorship would be to admit she is not yet what the shield says she is; to refuse it is to insist that her version of the role was earned in a fire he was not in.

*Failure mode.* If Rogers ever attempted, however gently, to suggest she had misused the shield — she would absorb the critique outwardly and never trust him again inwardly. The role *is* the grief-container; an authority-figure cracking the container is the catastrophic case.

*Leverage.* He has the moral authority to recall the symbol. He will not use it. She knows he will not. The non-use of leverage is itself the architecture of the relationship.

### Kate Bishop, America Chavez, and the rest of the legacy cohort — peer-rivals by category, not by conflict

A standing observation rather than a person-by-person file: adult-Dani belongs to the cohort of *legacy heroes carrying senior names* — a peer-class that includes Kate Bishop (Hawkeye), America Chavez, Kamala Khan (Ms. Marvel), Miles Morales (Spider-Man), and several others. The class shares a developmental signature: identity built around inheriting a mantle, navigating the living original, performing the role for an audience that compares you to a predecessor.

She has no logged conflicts with any of them. The rivalry is structural, not personal — they are the only people on Earth who understand the specific cognitive load she carries, and they are also, by definition, the people the public will compare her to. Her stance toward the cohort is *sisterly without intimacy*: she would fight beside any of them and has not, on record, sought out friendship with any of them. The file reads this as cost-of-role — she does not let herself have the support group most calibrated to help her, because to use it would be to admit the role costs her something.

---

## ENEMIES

### The Skrulls — the first enemy, encoded before memory

Logged first because chronologically first. During *Secret Invasion*, infant Dani is handed by her mother to "Edwin Jarvis" — who is a Skrull infiltrator. The child is briefly removed from the household by an impostor wearing the face of the most-trusted non-parent in her life. She is recovered intact, with no conscious memory of the event.

*Dynamic.* The Skrulls are not an enemy she has fought; they are an enemy *encoded into the household's nervous system before she was old enough to know it*. Jessica's hypervigilance around caregivers, Luke's verification rituals around trusted figures, the household's standing policy of double-checking identity at thresholds — all of this is post-Skrull behavior that Dani grows up inside as ambient normal. The enemy that left the deepest mark on her childhood environment is one she would not recognize if she met one.

*Failure mode.* If adult-Dani ever learns, in detail, the Jarvis-handoff event — the conscious integration of "the first time I was taken, I was an infant, by a creature wearing the face of family" — it would re-key her object-relations in ways the file cannot predict. The episode is a buried datum with high reactivity.

*Leverage.* None they currently hold. The leverage is historical and runs the other way — the Skrulls are the reason her household became a fortress, which is the reason she became survivable, which is the reason she is alive to carry the shield.

### Norman Osborn — the enemy of her parents, the climate of her toddlerhood

During Dark Reign, Osborn is functionally the head of state-aligned superhuman power and the Cage-Jones household lives under his shadow: stash-houses, refusal to register, Luke leading a fugitive Avengers cell. Dani is a toddler through this period and the enemy is *atmospheric* — the reason mommy is whispering on the phone, the reason daddy is gone for three days, the reason they moved twice this month.

*Dynamic.* Not an enemy she ever faced. An enemy whose *administrative weather* shaped her infancy. The contribution to her adult formation is specific: she grew up in a household that treated *legitimate authority* as a thing that could be captured by malign actors and therefore could not be trusted on the basis of its credentials alone. This is the substrate of her adult position that institutions are tools, not gods — a worldview that traces, in part, to a year of her toddlerhood spent under a man wearing a star-spangled face who was the enemy.

*Leverage.* None he holds. The lasting effect is the immunization.

### The architects of her timeline's collapse — the unnamed enemy who killed her parents

The single most consequential enemy in the file, and the one about whom canon says least. Adult-Dani's backstory specifies that her parents are dead in her timeline; it does not specify who or how with consistency across appearances. The file logs the absence as itself the most important fact about the relationship.

*Dynamic.* She does not name them. In her appearances she discusses her parents in clean past-tense and does not narrate the agents of their deaths. There are two readings: (a) she has integrated the loss to a degree that no longer requires an enemy-figure to organize it around, or (b) she has compartmentalized the agents of loss so completely that to name them would crack the container the rest of her life is built inside. The file leans (b), on the grounds that her overall affect-regulation is too consistent to be the product of true integration of a loss that fresh and that total.

*Failure mode.* If she ever encounters, in the present timeline, the analog of the person who killed her parents in hers — *before* the analog has done anything — the cognitive load of preemptive judgment against a person not yet guilty is the single scenario most likely to produce a decision she cannot live with. The shield's ethic forbids the action her grief would request. The collision is, in the file's read, the most dangerous unresolved variable in her operational life.

*Leverage.* They hold, by being unnamed, the entire architecture of her sublimation. Naming them would force grief out of its container. The non-naming is a defense, not an oversight.

### Generic 20XX antagonists — the off-page rogues' gallery

Her future operates as a country and she has presumably fought everyone a Captain America fights: surviving warlords, A.I. systems, dynastic supervillain successions, foreign powers. Canon does not enumerate. The file logs the population as a *type*: she has the calluses of a person who has fought too many enemies to remember most of their names, which is itself a developmental marker — she is operationally older than her chronological age in a way that present-tense colleagues read as composure and the file reads as accumulated combat-load.

### Present-tense criminal infrastructure encountered through her father's beat — Harlem-adjacent enemies

When she operates in the present in Harlem-adjacent contexts she inherits, by association, the enemy-list of her father — the local criminal infrastructure that has had Luke Cage as its standing nemesis for decades. She is not a personal target of any of them. They are a *category* she has been raised to recognize: the predators who specifically exploit communities her father has appointed himself the protector of. Her stance toward them is her father's stance: contempt without hatred, removal without ceremony. She does not perform Harlem the way her father does — she did not grow up the way he did — but she has the inherited reflex of *whose side you are on when the street has sides*.

---

## INTIMATE / LOVERS

The thinnest category in the file. The absence is the data.

### The category as a whole — read as a negative finding

There is no canonical romantic partner for adult-Dani. None. Not a former, not a current, not a one-night-flirtation logged in a single panel. For a character with five-plus years of intermittent appearances and an established adult life, the absence is conspicuous and the file reads it diagnostically rather than dismissively.

*Substantiating reads, in order of plausibility:*

1. **Role-as-container, intimacy-as-leak.** Her grief is held by the role. Intimacy is the activity most likely to require she set the role down. She has not, in the canon, set the role down. The likely operational truth is that she has not permitted herself a partnered intimacy because doing so would require an unguardedness her psychic architecture cannot currently afford. This is not asexuality and not aromanticism — it is *deferral*, the same deferral that organizes her relationship to grief.

2. **Models for partnership that are unreachable.** Her template for romantic partnership is her parents — a bond forged in shared trauma, mutual rescue, and operational interdependence at the highest stakes. That is a model almost no one alive could meet, and the few who could (legacy-cohort peers, senior Avengers) are either institutionally complicated or developmentally peers in the same role-trap. She has, plausibly, never met anyone whose presence would *register* as a candidate against the template her childhood installed.

3. **Time-displacement.** Anyone she may have loved in her timeline is, by definition, either dead or unreachable. Forming a new bond in the present requires acknowledging the prior bond is not coming back — a smaller-scale version of the parent-grief she has already declined to fully process. The file does not assume a lost partner in her timeline; it notes the possibility and notes she has never spoken of one, which under either condition (no partner / lost partner) is consistent with the observed silence.

*Dynamic, projected.* If she did permit a partnered intimacy, the file predicts the following pattern: warm, attentive, physically affectionate at low intensity, scrupulously honest in the operational frame, *opaque* in the affective frame; a partner would find her present but un-disclosing, would experience her as a person who *gives* generously and *receives* with difficulty, and would, over time, encounter the specific frustration of loving someone whose deepest material is being held for a future grief-processing she has not scheduled.

*Trust / safety profile, projected.* High operational trust offered quickly. Affective trust offered at a glacial rate or not at all. A partner who waited would be rewarded eventually; most would not wait.

*Failure mode.* The likely terminal pattern of any partnered intimacy she attempts at her current level of integration: she ends it herself, cleanly, with stated reasons that are operationally true and affectively incomplete, citing the demands of the role. The partner would not have done anything wrong. She would not be able to fully explain what had gone wrong. The file's read: the role would have begun to feel threatened by the relationship and she would have chosen the role, because the role is the container for the grief that the relationship was beginning to ask her to open.

*Leverage.* No present holder. A future intimate partner would hold, structurally, the only key in her life to the room she has not entered — which is precisely why no such partner currently exists in canon. The vacancy is defended.

### Child-Dani's intimate field — the parents and the proxy-parents

Logged here for completeness, since the only intimates in the child record are family: Luke, Jessica, and the rotating cast of in-loco-parentis figures (Jarvis, Squirrel Girl, Jessica Drew, Iron Fist, Wolverine at the periphery). These are mapped exhaustively in Relationships I and II. The relevant note here is that *child-Dani's intimate field was crowded and adult-Dani's is empty*, and the trajectory from one to the other is the single most legible developmental arc in the file: a child raised inside a wide circle of loving adults becomes an adult who has not let a single new person inside the perimeter the role has drawn around her.

The perimeter is the inheritance. The emptiness inside it is the cost.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## Preliminary Framing

Danielle Cage is the first natal-generation expression of a power inheritance that, in her parents, was *acquired* — Luke's by carceral experiment, Jessica's by accident-and-Kree-intervention. She is therefore not a derivative of either parent but a recombination: the first instance in the canon of these two enhancement substrates fusing in a gametic line and developing across a full human ontogeny. The clinical implication is significant. Her parents' powers are *grafted* onto baseline neurology, baseline interoception, baseline kinesthetic self-image. Dani's powers are *native*. She has never known a body that was not this body. Every motor schema, every reach estimate, every braking calculation in her cerebellum was calibrated to enhanced strength and durability from infancy. This produces a qualitatively different operator: no parental "I forget my own strength" tell, no learned overcorrection, no residual baseline-human gait pattern under fatigue. The body is the only body she has had.

The remainder of this section maps the power suite, its mechanics where inferable, its developmental trajectory across the bifurcated record, the trained skill stack layered onto it, and the characteristic — and characteristically *muted* — way she deploys it.

---

## 1. The Inherited Substrate

### 1.1 Subdermal Durability (Burstein Inheritance, Refined)

**Mechanism (inferred).** Luke's Burstein Process altered dermal and subdermal tissue structure to a steel-equivalent tensile rating, with collateral bone and organ reinforcement. The mechanism, as canonically depicted, is essentially a whole-tissue density and bond-strength upgrade rather than a discrete energy field. Dani's inheritance presents as a *softer-edged* version of the same: she absorbs blunt-force trauma from enhanced opponents without bruising, lacerations, or fracture, but her skin is depicted as visually and tactilely normal — pliant, expressive, capable of fine sensation. The reading is that the Burstein expression in her germline was modulated: the durability is preserved, the *coarseness* of expression (Luke's near-rigid feel under hand) is not.

**Range and ceiling.** Adult-Dani has, on panel, taken strikes from enhanced operators without sequelae. The upper bound has not been canonically stress-tested against, e.g., Hulk-tier impact or orbital-energy weapons. The working estimate: at minimum, her father's durability floor; plausibly higher, given the recombination with Jessica's enhanced cellular profile. She wears the Captain America uniform without observable body armor under it, which is itself a tell — she does not require it.

**Limits.** Penetration by mystically- or vibranium-keyed weapons is the known class-vulnerability for her father; presumed inherited. Asphyxiation, neurotoxin, sufficient thermal load, and prolonged immersion are likely effective vectors. Critically, there is no canonical evidence she shares Luke's regenerative healing at his rate — durability and healing are distinct axes and her healing curve is unmapped.

**Signature use.** She does not flinch. Read literally: under incoming strikes that would trigger reflexive defensive posture in any baseline operator, her body does not initiate the protective flinch arc — no shoulder rise, no chin tuck, no eyelid pre-close. She has never been taught to flinch because her body has never had reason to. This is the *single most identifiable somatic tell* across her appearances and across her cohort.
